Mu'tazila Scriptural Proofs for Capability Preceding Action
Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila's scriptural proofs from two Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila use Quranic verses to argue that capability (istita'a) must exist before the action.

Core Idea
The Quran commands with conditional capability, implying capability precedes action.

The Mu'tazila begin their argument for the precedence of capability by citing two Quranic verses. These verses appear to show that capability is a prerequisite for the commanded action, not a consequence of it. This lesson examines their scriptural reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the two Quranic proofs
Read the Arabic passage containing the two verses and the Mu'tazila's interpretation. Note how they derive the precedence of capability.
2
Analyze the first verse (64:16)
Consider how 'as much as you are able' is interpreted to mean that the one commanded must already have the capability.
The Mu'tazila argue that the verse imposes a condition of capability on the command.
3
Analyze the second verse (7:171)
See how the analogy of taking with strength implies the strength must precede the taking.
They use a logical analogy of hand and taking.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why these scriptural proofs are foundational for the Mu'tazila position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاحتجت المعتزلة بقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَاتَّقُوا اللَّهَ مَا اسْتَطَعْتُمْ ﴾ فينبغي أن يكون كل من لزمه التقوى مع عدم التقوى، إذ غير المتقي لزمه التقوى، فينبغي أن يكون معه استطاعة التقوى وفي وجود استطاعة التقوى ولا تقوى، قول بتقدم استطاعة التقوى على التقوى. واحتجوا أيضا بقوله: ﴿ خُذُوا مَا آتَيْنَاكُم بِقُوَّةٍ ﴾ والأخذ بقوة لن يتصور أن تكون القوة سابقة على الأخذ كالأخذ باليد لن يتصور إلا وأن تكون اليد سابقة عليه. والمعقول لهم أن القدرة لو لم تكن سابقة على الفعل ولم تكن مع الكافر قدرة الإيمان سابقة على الإيمان لكان الأمر له بالإيمان محالاً لما فيه من تكليف ما لا يطاق، وهو قبيح في بديهة العقل فإن من أمر عبده المقعد بالعدو وعبده الأعمى بالنظر يعد سفيهًا خارجًا عن الحكمة. ولأن قدرة الإيجاد يستحيل تعلقها بالموجود لما في إيجاد الموجود من الاستحالة فإذا هي تتعلق بالمعدوم ليوجد بها، وإنما تكون قدرة على المعدوم دون الموجود أن لو كانت سابقة عليه، فأما إذا كانت مقارنة للفعل فهي متعلقة بالموجود وهو محال. ولأن القدرة لو كانت مقارنة للفعل لم تكن القدرة بكونها علة لوجود الفعل أولى من القلب إذ خروجها من العدم إلى الوجود مطابقا فلا يمكن إضافة وجود أحدهما إلى الآخر بل أضيف وجودهما جميعًا إلى غيرهما، ولو أضيف وجود الفعل إلى القدرة مع استواء حالهما لجاز إضافة وجود القدرة إلى الفعل. ولأنه لو كان لا يؤمن حتى يقدر ولا يقدر حتى يؤمن يبقى أبدًا غير مؤمن كالواقع في البشر إذا كان لا يخرج حتى يأتيه بالحبل ولا يأتيه بالحبل حتى يخرج لم يخرج أبدًا. ولأن الكافر لو لم يكن معه قدرة الإيمان لكان معذورًا ولم يكن تعذيبه عدلاً، إذ لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول: لو قيل له لم لم تفعل كذا؟ فيقول: لأني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب. ويسألون ويقولون هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها مراقبة الله تعالى؟ فإن قلتم: لا، فقد أعظمتم القول في وصف الأنبياء عليهم السلام، وينبغي أن لا يثاب في الاتقاء عن المعاصي وإن قلتم: نعم، فقد أقررتم بوجود الاستطاعة ولا فعل.
The Mu'tazila argued by His saying, the Exalted: 'So fear Allah as much as you are able' (64:16). So it should be that everyone who is obligated to have taqwa while lacking taqwa—since the non-muttaqi is obligated to have taqwa—should have the capability (istita'a) of taqwa. And with the existence of the capability of taqwa and no taqwa, [this] is a statement of the precedence of the capability of taqwa over taqwa. They also argued by His saying: 'Take what We have given you with strength' (7:171). And taking with strength cannot be conceived unless strength precedes the taking, just as taking by hand cannot be conceived unless the hand precedes it. Their rational [argument] is that if power (qudra) were not prior to the action and the disbeliever did not have the power of faith prior to faith, then the command to him to believe would be impossible because it involves commanding that which cannot be borne, which is ugly in the immediate judgment of reason. For whoever commands his paralyzed slave to run and his blind slave to see is considered foolish, outside of wisdom. And because the power of bringing into existence cannot attach to what exists due to the impossibility of bringing into existence what exists; rather, it attaches to what is non-existent so that it may be brought into existence by it. It is only a power over the non-existent, not the existent, if it is prior to it. But if it is concomitant with the action, then it is attached to what exists, which is impossible. And because if power were concomitant with the action, power would not be more deserving of being a cause for the existence of the action than the action, since both emerge from non-existence into existence in a similar manner. So the existence of one cannot be attributed to the other; rather, the existence of both is attributed to something else. And if the existence of the action were attributed to power while both are equal, then it would be permissible to attribute the existence of power to the action. And because if one does not believe until he has power and does not have power until he believes, he would remain forever a non-believer, like a man in a well who does not come out until he gets a rope and does not get a rope until he comes out—he would never come out. And because if the disbeliever did not have the power of faith, he would be excused and his punishment would not be just. For there is no greater excuse for a slave in the witnessed world than to say, when asked 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', 'Because I was not able to do it.' So likewise in the unseen. And they ask: Did anyone ever refrain from disobeying Allah the Exalted while being capable of it out of mindfulness of Allah? If you say 'No,' then you have made a grave statement regarding the description of the prophets, peace be upon them, and they should not be rewarded for refraining from sins. And if you say 'Yes,' then you have acknowledged the existence of capability without action.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's arguments for the proposition that capability (istita'a) precedes the action. The passage is a continuous presentation of multiple proofs. The author, Imam Nasafi, is reporting the arguments of the Mu'tazila without yet refuting them. The first proof is scriptural: from Quran 64:16, 'So fear Allah as much as you are able.' The Mu'tazila argue that since the command to have taqwa (piety) is addressed to everyone, including those who lack taqwa, and the command is conditional upon capability (ma istata'tum), the one commanded must have the capability for taqwa even before taqwa actually exists. Thus capability precedes the action. The second proof is also scriptural: from Quran 7:171, 'Take what We have given you with strength.' They argue that taking with strength (quwwa) cannot be conceived unless the strength precedes the taking, just as taking by hand requires the hand to be prior. Next, they present a rational argument: if power (qudra) were not prior to the action, and the disbeliever did not have the power of faith prior to his faith, then commanding him to believe would be commanding the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), which is rationally ugly (qabih). They illustrate this with an analogy: a master who commands a paralyzed slave to run or a blind slave to see is considered foolish. Another argument is based on the nature of power of bringing into existence (qudrat al-ijad). It cannot attach to something already existing, because bringing into existence what exists is impossible. Therefore, it must attach to a non-existent thing in order to bring it into existence, which requires that the power precede the action. If it were concomitant, it would attach to something already existing, which is impossible. Another argument concerns causation: if power were concomitant with the action, then neither would be more deserving to be the cause of the other, since both come into existence simultaneously. Thus one cannot attribute the existence of the action to power without allowing the reverse. Another analogy: if a person's belief requires prior power and that power requires prior belief, there would be an infinite regress, like a man in a well who needs a rope to get out but needs to get out to get the rope. Finally, they argue from justice: if the disbeliever lacked the power of faith, he would be excused and punishment would be unjust. In worldly affairs, a person who says 'I was not able' is excused. So likewise in the afterlife. They then pose a rhetorical question: did any prophet ever refrain from sin while being capable of it out of mindfulness of Allah? If you say no, you impugn the prophets; if yes, you admit that capability exists without action, supporting their view that capability precedes action.

Key Terms
استطاعة (capability)
تقوى (piety)
قوة (strength)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila's scriptural proofs are the author's own position.
Correction: Imam Nasafi is merely reporting the Mu'tazila's arguments; he will refute them later.

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the Mu'tazila use to argue that capability precedes the action?
A) 64:16
B) 7:171
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila interpret Quranic verses to show that capability must logically precede the action it enables.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila argue that a command implies the commanded person already has the capability. When you hear Allah's commands, do you truly believe you have the capability to obey them, or do you rely on excuses that 'it's too hard'? Which command are you currently excusing yourself from, and what would it mean to act as if you already have the capability?
?
The verse says 'as much as you are able' — but do you actually gauge your obedience by your current ability, or do you secretly hold back, fearing that obedience will cost you something you love? What would you do today if you truly believed that Allah has already given you the capability to do it?
?
If capability truly precedes action, then your failure to obey is not a lack of ability but a choice. What does that say about the excuses you make for your sins? Will you confront the choice you are making right now, or will you continue to hide behind 'I can't'?

Mu'tazila Rational Proofs for Capability Preceding Action
Nasafi reports the Mu'tazila's rational arguments for capability preceding action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments the Mu'tazila present for the precedence of capability over action.

Core Idea
Without prior capability, commanding an action would be command of the impossible.

After citing scriptural proofs, the Mu'tazila present several rational arguments. These include the impossibility of commanding what cannot be done, the nature of creative power, causation, infinite regress, and justice. This lesson unpacks these rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational arguments
The passage contains multiple rational proofs. Read them carefully.
2
Analyze the 'commanding the impossible' argument
Consider that if the disbeliever lacks prior power, commanding faith would be commanding the impossible, which is rationally ugly.
This is a key point.
3
Understand the nature of power argument
Power of bringing into existence cannot attach to what exists; it must precede.
Logical reasoning.
4
Examine the causation and infinite regress arguments
If power is concomitant, neither is cause of the other, and belief would never occur.
Analogies like the well man illustrate.
5
Reflect on the justice argument
Without prior power, the disbeliever would be excused, making punishment unjust.
Consider worldly analogy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And because the power of bringing into existence cannot attach to what exists due to the impossibility of bringing into existence what exists; rather, it attaches to what is non-existent so that it may be brought into existence by it. So the existence of one cannot be attributed to the other; rather, the existence of both is attributed to something else. Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's arguments for the proposition that capability (istita'a) precedes the action. The passage is a continuous presentation of multiple proofs. The author, Imam Nasafi, is reporting the arguments of the Mu'tazila without yet refuting them. The first proof is scriptural: from Quran 64:16, 'So fear Allah as much as you are able.' They argue that taking with strength (quwwa) cannot be conceived unless the strength precedes the taking, just as taking by hand requires the hand to be prior. Next, they present a rational argument: if power (qudra) were not prior to the action, and the disbeliever did not have the power of faith prior to his faith, then commanding him to believe would be commanding the impossible (taklif ma la yutaq), which is rationally ugly (qabih). They illustrate this with an analogy: a master who commands a paralyzed slave to run or a blind slave to see is considered foolish. Another argument is based on the nature of power of bringing into existence (qudrat al-ijad). It cannot attach to something already existing, because bringing into existence what exists is impossible. If it were concomitant, it would attach to something already existing, which is impossible. Another argument concerns causation: if power were concomitant with the action, then neither would be more deserving to be the cause of the other, since both come into existence simultaneously. Thus one cannot attribute the existence of the action to power without allowing the reverse. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of Allah that feels overwhelming, do you ever tell yourself it's impossible for you? How does the Mu'tazila argument that God does not command the impossible expose the weakness of that excuse?
?
The Mu'tazila claim that without prior power, the disbeliever would be excused. Have you ever used 'I couldn't help it' to justify a sin? What does this passage imply about the accountability you must accept?
?
The man in the well remains stuck because he waits for the rope before climbing. Is there an area of your deen where you are waiting for a condition (like feeling capable) before acting? What would it look like to break that cycle and act now, trusting that capability comes with action?

intermediate
8 min
The Impossibility of Power Preceding the Action
Al-Nasafi proves power must be simultaneous with action via the non-endurance of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim theologians argue that human power (qudra) must be simultaneous with the action, not preceding it.

Core Idea
Power is an accident that cannot endure, so it must coincide with the action.

Al-Nasafi engages a key debate in Islamic theology: can human power exist before the act? His argument rests on the nature of accidents and their inability to persist. By tracing the logical consequences of power preceding the act, he shows it leads to absurdity—confusing the capable with the incapable. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Read the opening sentence: power is an accident that does not endure. This is the foundation of the entire argument.
Accidents are temporal; they cannot remain for two moments.
2
Follow the conditional argument
If power precedes and does not endure, it would be absent when the action occurs. What happens then? Power only ceases to incapacity, so the agent would be incapable. Yet the action occurs—a contradiction.
Notice the chain: no power → incapacity → action from incapable is absurd.
3
Grasp the reductio ad absurdum
Al-Nasafi takes it further: if action were possible without power (and impossible with it), then power and incapacity would swap definitions, which is self-defeating.
This shows the logical necessity of simultaneity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاستطاعة عرض يكون مع الفعل إن القدرة لما كانت غير قابلة للبقاء، وساعدنا عليه كثير من المعتزلة، وأثبتنا ذلك بالدليل على من أبي القول، ولو كانت هي متقدمة على الفعل ولا بقاء إلى الثاني من الأوقات كانت عدمًا وقت وجود الفعل، فيوجد الفعل ولا قدرة، وهي لا تنعدم عمن يجوز قيامها به إلا عما يضادها وهو العجز، ومن انعدمت قدرته يستحيل اتصافه بكونه قادرًا، ومن قام به العجز يستحيل أن لا يكون عاجزا، فعلى هذا التقدير يكون الفعل مستحيل الوجود من القادر واجب الوجود من العاجز، ومن هذا قوله، فهو قد أصيب بعقله ولم يشعر. يحققه أن الفعل لو جاز وجوده مع انعدام القدرة بل وجب هي لا تنعدم إلا إلى عجز، واستحال وجوده مع وجودها لكانت القدرة هي العجز والعجز هو القدرة، فإنا لا نعرف العجز إلا بأن يستحيل معه الفعل والقدرة إلا بأن يجب بها الفعل ويثبت.
The capacity is an accident that is with the action. Indeed, power (al-qudra) when it is not susceptible to remaining, and many of the Mu'tazila agreed with us on it, and we proved that by evidence against whoever refused the statement. And if it were preceding the action and no remaining to the second of the times, it would be non-existence at the time of the existence of the action, so the action occurs and no power. And it (power) does not cease from one for whom it is possible to subsist in him except by that which opposes it, which is incapacity (al-ʿajz). And whoever's power ceases, it is impossible to describe him as being capable (qādir). And whoever has incapacity subsisting in him, it is impossible that he not be incapable. So according to this estimation, the action would be impossible of existence from the capable and necessary of existence from the incapable. And from this is his statement, so he has been stricken in his intellect and does not feel. He verifies that if the action were permissible of existence with the non-existence of power—rather it is obligatory—power does not cease except into incapacity, and its existence with its existence is impossible, then power would be incapacity and incapacity would be power. For we do not know incapacity except by the action being impossible with it, and power except by the action being necessary by it and established.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the human capacity (al-istitaʿa, also called al-qudra) must be simultaneous with the action and cannot precede it. Al-Nasafi begins by stating the premise that power is an accident (ʿarad) that does not endure (lā taqbil al-baqāʾ). He notes that many Muʿtazila agree with this premise. He argues: if power were to precede the action and not endure to the second moment, it would be non-existent at the time of the action. Thus the action occurs without power. But power does not cease from a person except through its opposite, which is incapacity (al-ʿajz). So if power is absent, incapacity must be present. A person whose power ceases cannot be described as capable; one in whom incapacity subsists cannot be not incapable. Therefore, on this scenario, the action would be impossible from the capable and necessary from the incapable—an absurdity. Al-Nasafi then reinforces this by noting that if action could exist when power is absent (and must, because power goes to incapacity) while action cannot exist when power is present, then power and incapacity would be indistinguishable: the defining characteristic of incapacity (impossibility of action) would be present with power, and the defining characteristic of power (necessity of action) would be absent. This confusion shows the impossibility of power preceding the action.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human power is a permanent quality that can exist before the act.
Correction: This passage shows that power is a transient accident that must coincide with the act; otherwise, absence of power implies incapacity, making action impossible.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's argument, what would happen if power (qudra) preceded the action and did not endure?
A) The action would occur with power present.
B) The action would occur without power, and incapacity would be present.
C) Power would remain until the action occurs.
D) The action would be impossible regardless.

Key Takeaway
Power must coincide with the action because as a non-enduring accident, its absence would entail incapacity, making action from the incapable absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel confident in your ability to do something, do you ever assume that ability is a permanent part of you, existing independently from the moment of action? How does al‑Nasafi’s proof challenge that assumption? Commit to one area of your life where you will consciously remind yourself that your power is a transient gift from Allah each moment.
?
The proof shows that if power were to precede the action, it would collapse into incapacity, making action impossible. In your own life, have you ever delayed an act of obedience, thinking your intention or resolve is enough ‘power’ to do it later? What does this passage teach you about the necessity of acting now, with the power Allah gives you at this very instant? Decide one good deed you will perform immediately after this lesson.
?
Al‑Nasafi calls those who separate power from action ‘stricken in intellect.’ Search your heart: do you ever attribute your successes solely to your own planning or previous abilities, forgetting that the actual power to act is created anew with each action? How can you integrate the awareness that your power is simultaneous with your action into your daily reliance on Allah? Write a short dua you will say before every action today to acknowledge this truth.

The Uselessness of Power If Action Exists Without It
Al-Nasafi challenges the necessity of power if action exists without it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's reductio ad absurdum argument that if action can occur without power, then affirming power is futile and denying it is more consistent.

Core Idea
If action exists without power, power is useless; deny it entirely.

In this passage, al-Nasafi presses the Mu'tazilite position to its logical extreme. If they claim power can precede the action without causing it, then the action must still occur even without power. This makes power superfluous. Al-Nasafi then challenges them to simply deny power altogether, which would be a more rational stance.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the argument
Recall that the Mu'tazilites assert power exists before the action without causing it. This argument challenges that view.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the rhetorical questions posed by al-Nasafi.
Focus on the phrase 'So say: There is no power at all.'
3
Identify the logical chain
Recognize that if action is necessary even without power, then power serves no purpose. Al-Nasafi concludes that denying power is more fitting.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that this is a reductio ad absurdum: the Mu'tazilite view leads to the absurd denial of human power.
This does not mean al-Nasafi denies human power; he is refuting their specific position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم: لما كان الفعل متصور الوجود مع انعدام القدرة بل هو واجب الوجود في هذه الحالة، فأي فائدة لوجود القدرة؟ وأي حاجة إليها؟ وأي أثر لوجودها سابقة على الفعل ولا تعلق له بها ويجب وجوده مع عدمها؟ فقولوا لا قدرة البتة إذ لا يقول بوجودها من يقول إلا لضرورة حصول الفعل بها كان يستحيل وجوده بها، ويجب وجوده مع عدمها أو ثبوت ضدها، كان القول بنفيها أصلاً أحق وأصوب.
Then it is said to them: Since the action is conceived of existence with the non-existence of power—rather it is necessary of existence in this state—what benefit is there for the existence of power? And what need is there for it? And what effect does its existence have when it is prior to the action and it has no connection to it, and its (the action's) existence is necessary with its non-existence? So say: There is no power at all, since no one asserts its existence except due to the necessity of the action occurring by it. So if it is impossible for the action to occur by it, and its existence is necessary with its non-existence or the establishment of its opposite, then the statement of its denial altogether is more fitting and more correct.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage presents a second argument against the Muʿtazilite position that power can precede the action. Al-Nasafi poses a rhetorical challenge: if an action can be conceived as existing when power is absent—indeed, it is necessary in that state—then what good is the existence of power? What need is there for it? What impact could a power that precedes the action have, since it has no connection to the action and the action must occur without it? The logical conclusion is that one should simply deny the existence of power altogether. This is because the only reason anyone affirms power is that it is necessary for the action to occur. But if the action occurs without it, or even with its opposite (incapacity), then affirming power serves no purpose. Denying power entirely is more consistent. This argument aims to show that the Muʿtazilite view leads to the elimination of the concept of human power, which is absurd.

عدم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Nasafi's argument denies human power entirely.
Correction: This is a reductio ad absurdum against the Muʿtazilite position that power can precede action without causal connection. Al-Nasafi does not deny human power; he shows that the Muʿtazilite premise leads to the absurd conclusion that power is useless.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does al-Nasafi draw from the assumption that action can exist without power?
A) Power must still exist but is ineffective.
B) It is better to deny power altogether.
C) Power is necessary for the action.
D) The action is impossible without power.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite position that power precedes action without causal connection leads to the absurd conclusion that power is useless, so denying it is more rational.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believe that your actions depend completely on Allah's will and power at every moment, does that make you more grateful or more anxious? What does your immediate reaction reveal about your heart's reliance?
?
This argument shows that claiming independent power leads to absurdity. In your daily life, where do you subtly assert independence from Allah—in your plans, your health, or your deeds? Will you repent from that false sense of power today?

The Necessity of Instruments and the Absurdity of Action Without Power
Al-Nasafi argues that deficient power requires instruments, and action without power contradicts divine tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why created power requires instruments and why action without simultaneous power would lead to absurd consequences that undermine the proof of God's power.

Core Idea
Action without power would make the universe evidence that God is not capable.

Al-Nasafi here tackles the Mu'tazilite claim that power may precede the action, arguing that action can only occur with simultaneous power. He presents two arguments: the first uses the necessity of instruments for deficient power to prove that action without power is impossible; the second shows that if action could occur without power, it would reverse the standard inference from action to agent's power, thereby undermining tawhid. Read carefully to follow these subtle but crucial logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two arguments marked by 'thumma naqūlu' and 'wa-alladhī yuqarriru dhālik'.
2
Understand the first argument
Identify why created power is deficient and why instruments are necessary. Then follow the analogy: if action without power were possible, it would also be possible without instruments, leading to absurdities.
The absurdities (grasping without hand etc.) show the impossibility.
3
Examine the second argument
Al-Nasafi argues that if power does not cause action when present but action occurs when power is absent, then the cause of action is the absence of power. This would make action evidence that the agent is not capable.
This reverses the standard kalam proof of God's power from the existence of the world.
4
Connect to tawhid
Realize that accepting the Mu'tazilite view would invalidate one of the main arguments for God's power, thus undermining tawhid. Also consider the point about power being useless if it exists without effect.
This shows the high stakes of this theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول: لما كانت القدرة المحدثة ناقصة ولم يكن بد من وجود الآلات والأسباب لتكمله هي، إذ الآلة أعدت لتكميل القدرة الناقصة، ولهذا يستحيل أن يكون الله تعالى فاعلاً بالآلة؛ لأن قدرته كاملة غير ناقصة فكانت مستغنية عما يكمل به، ثم لو جاز وجود الفعل مع انعدام حقيقة القدرة جاز أيضا مع انعدام ما أعد تتميمًا لها فيقبض الإنسان حال عدم اليد، ويمشي حال عدم الرجل، ويقطع حال عدم السكين، وحيث استحال ذلك وإن كانت هذه الآلات موجودة قبل ذلك بلا فصل فكذا في حقيقة القدرة. وفساد هذا مما لا يخفى على من رزق أدنى لب، فكذا هذا. والذي يقرر ذلك : أن القدرة لو لم يكن بها فعل وهي موجودة ويكون بها وهي غير موجودة فيكون سبب وجود الفعل عدم القدرة لا وجودها، فيصير القول به قولا بوجود الفعل لعدم القدرة، فيكون الفعل دليلاً على أن ليس الفاعل بقادر ولا على أنه قادر، وبالفعل استدلوا على أن الله تعالى قادر، فبطل موضع الاستدلال بالشاهد ووقع على العكس، وكان ينبغي أن يستدل بوجود الفعل على أن الفاعل ليس بقادر، وفيه بطلان القول بالتوحيد، ولأن القدرة إذا كانت لا تنفع وهي موجودة، فوجودها وقت الوجود وعدمها بمنزلة كانت أولى أن لا تنفع وهي معدومة، وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالفعل من غير القدرة وإهدارها، وهذا محال.
Then we say: Since created power (al-qudra al-muḥdatha) is deficient, and there must be the existence of instruments and causes to complete it—for the instrument is prepared to complete the deficient power—therefore it is impossible for God Most High to act by an instrument, because His power is perfect, not deficient, so it is independent of what completes it. Then if the existence of the action with the non-existence of the reality of power were permissible, it would also be permissible with the non-existence of what is prepared as a completion for it. So a person would grasp when the hand is absent, and walk when the leg is absent, and cut when the knife is absent. And since that is impossible, even if these instruments existed before that without interval, so too for the reality of power. And the corruption of this is not hidden from one who has been given even the least intellect, so too this. And what settles that is that if power, there is no action with it when it exists, and there is action with it when it does not exist—then the cause of the existence of the action is the non-existence of power, not its existence. So the statement becomes a statement of the existence of the action due to the non-existence of power. Thus the action becomes evidence that the agent is not capable and not that he is capable. And by action they have inferred that God Most High is capable. So the place of inference from the witnessed is invalidated and it occurs conversely, and it would be necessary to infer from the existence of the action that the agent is not capable, and in that is the invalidation of the statement of tawḥīd. And because power, if it is of no benefit when it exists, then its existence at the time of existence and its non-existence are equivalent—it is more fitting that it be of no benefit when it is non-existent. And in that is the necessary consequence of stating action without power and disregarding it, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage contains two interconnected arguments. First, al-Nasafi argues from the deficiency of human power: created power (al-qudra al-muḥdatha) is inherently deficient and requires instruments (ālāt) and causes to complete it. Instruments are prepared precisely to complete deficient power. God's power is perfect and therefore independent of instruments—hence God cannot be said to act through instruments. Now, if the Muʿtazilite claim that action can occur without the reality of power were true, then by analogy, action should also be possible without the instruments that complete power. This would lead to absurdities like grasping without a hand, walking without a leg, or cutting without a knife. Since those are impossible even when the instruments existed just before, the same holds for power itself—power must exist at the moment of action. The second argument (beginning with 'wa-alladhī yuqarriru dhālik') reinforces: if power exists with no action and is absent when action occurs, then the cause of the action is the absence of power. This would make the action evidence that the agent is not capable—contrary to the standard kalam argument that action in the witnessed world proves the agent's power. It would reverse the inference, and when applied to God, it would imply that existence of the universe proves God is not capable, undermining tawḥīd. Moreover, if power is useless when present, its presence and absence are equivalent, so it is more fitting that it be useless when absent, leading to the conclusion that action occurs without power, which is impossible (muḥāl).

Key Terms

آلة
نقص
تمام
فعل
توحيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-Nasafi's argument denies the need for instruments in human actions.
Correction: The argument uses the necessity of instruments to prove the necessity of simultaneous power, not to deny instruments. It argues that if action were possible without power, it would also be possible without instruments, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Nasafi argue that action cannot occur without power, using the example of instruments?
A) Because power is like an instrument for God.
B) Because if action could occur without power, it could also occur without instruments, which is absurd.
C) Because instruments are more important than power.
D) Because power is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Action without simultaneous power is impossible because it would entail absurd analogies and reverse the evidence for God's power, undermining tawhid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your own power is created and deficient, requiring instruments to complete it, do you live with a sense of utter dependence on Allah for every action, or do you take your abilities for granted? This lesson forces you to see that even your smallest action yesterday—whether a kind word or a turn of your hand—required power that Allah loaned you at that moment. Will you thank Him specifically for each action today?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that claiming action without power would make the evidence point to God not being capable. In your own life, when you fail to act on a good intention—like praying on time or helping someone—do you excuse yourself by saying 'I couldn't' without examining whether you truly used the power Allah gave you? What would it mean to treat every moment of ability as a direct gift from the All-Capable?
?
The absurdity of grasping without a hand reveals that power and instrument must coexist. But spiritually, do you ever try to 'grasp' blessings—like closeness to Allah—without using the instruments He provided (e.g., prayer, fasting, charity)? This passage calls you to examine: Are you using the instruments of faith that complete your deficient power, or are you expecting results without the means?

Nasafi Exposes the Mu'tazila's Contradiction: Denying Power Leads to Jabr
Nasafi argues that denying power at the time of the act implies compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazila position on human power leads to the very doctrine of compulsion (jabr) they reject.

Core Idea
Denying power at the moment of action makes every act compulsory — a doctrine the Mu'tazila claim to oppose.

In this passage, Nasafi delivers a sharp critique of the Mu'tazila, showing that their denial of human power at the time of the act logically forces them into the very position they reject: jabr, or divine compulsion. By drawing an analogy with the loss of physical tools, he demonstrates that an act occurring without power is inherently coercive. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the Maturidi critique of Mu'tazili qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it, then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the compact style; each clause builds on the previous.
2
Identify the key analogy
Nasafi compares the absence of power to the loss of physical tools (ālat). Observe how he uses 'Do you not see...' to draw the analogy.
The analogy strengthens the point: if loss of tools forces compulsion, absence of power is even more compelling.
3
Trace the logical consequence
Follow how Nasafi moves from analogy to conclusion: the Mu'tazila, by denying power at the moment, have effectively made the act occur by compulsion, yet they still hold the servant accountable – that is jabr.
This reveals the internal contradiction in Mu'tazila thought.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: why is this criticism devastating for the Mu'tazila? It shows they cannot consistently deny jabr while denying power at the time of action.
This argument is central to the Maturidi position on kasb.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبين سفه المعتزلة وجهلهم بالحقائق أن الفعل لو كان يقع ولا قدرة عليه للفاعل وقت وقوعه لكان وقوعه عن اضطرار، ألا يُرَى أنه لو وقع بعد ارتفاع صحة الآلات لكان واقعًا عن اضطرار، فكذا هذا. بل أولى إذ حصول الفعل بالقدرة دون صحة الأسباب. ثم إن المعتزلة صيروا العهد بهذا الفصل الواقع عن اضطرار وليا لله تعالى وعدوا له وهذا هو القول بالجبر.
And among what shows the foolishness of the Mu'tazila and their ignorance of realities is that if the act were to occur with no power over it for the agent at the time of its occurrence, then its occurrence would be by compulsion (idṭirār). Do you not see that if it occurred after the removal of the soundness of the tools, it would occur by compulsion? So likewise this. Rather it is more fitting, because the act occurs by power without the soundness of the causes. Then the Mu'tazila made the servant, by this chapter occurring by compulsion, a friend of Allah and an enemy to Him, and this is the statement of jabr (compulsion).
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an exposure of the Mu'tazila's inconsistency. Nasafi argues that if the act occurs without power at the moment, it is by compulsion (idṭirār), just as an act would be compulsory if it occurred after the loss of limbs/tools. Moreover, the Mu'tazila themselves, by holding that the act is not caused by power at that moment, have effectively made the servant's act occur by compulsion, yet they attribute that act to the servant as a basis for friendship or enmity with God—which is exactly the doctrine of jabr they claim to reject. Thus they contradict themselves.

Key Terms
اضطرار
جبر
قدرة
آلات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jabr only occurs if God directly compels a person against his will.
Correction: Nasafi shows that if a person lacks power at the moment of the act, the act is compulsory even without direct divine compulsion. Thus jabr can arise from metaphysical absence of power, not just overt coercion.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what does the Mu'tazila's denial of power at the time of the act ultimately lead to?
A) The act being voluntary.
B) The act being by compulsion (jabr).
C) The act being impossible.
D) The act being caused by the tool.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazila position on human power logically leads to the very doctrine of compulsion they seek to avoid, thus exposing their internal contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Read the source passage again. Consider times when you felt your actions were not truly your own—perhaps due to habit or pressure. Did you ever use that feeling as an excuse to evade responsibility? How does Nasafi's exposure of the Mu'tazila's contradiction challenge you to rethink the relationship between your power and your actions before Allah?
?
The Mu'tazila claimed to uphold human agency but inadvertently affirmed compulsion. In what areas of your life might you be clinging to a position that sounds noble but, upon honest reflection, undermines your accountability to Allah? What would Nasafi say to you?
?
If powerlessness at the moment of action makes every act compulsory, then your every sin is either a choice made with power or a compelled act. Which is it? If you believe you had no power when you sinned, does that mean God forced you? How does this lesson force you to answer?

Refuting Mu'tazila: Their View on Power Implies Compulsion
Refutes Mu'tazila by showing their view entails compulsion and they are true Jabriyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila position on power (qudra) and proves that their own view entails compulsion (jabr), making them the true Jabriyya.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila inadvertently endorse the compulsion they deny.

Imam al-Nasafi exposes a fatal flaw in the Mu'tazila theory of power. He shows that by denying that power exists at the moment of action, the Mu'tazila cannot distinguish their position from outright compulsion. In fact, their view actually makes all acts occur by force, which contradicts their claim to defend free will. This passage powerfully turns the tables on the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
Review the Mu'tazila view that power (qudra) must exist before the act, not at the same time. This is essential background for the refutation.
The passage begins by critiquing this exact claim.
2
Read the first argument: power absence makes no difference
Read the opening sentence carefully. Nasafi argues that if power is not present at the time of the act, then it makes no difference whether it exists before or after — both are absent when needed. Since power after the act is absurd, power before the act (as the Mu'tazila claim) is equally absurd.
This is a reductio ad absurdum, showing the Mu'tazila premise leads to an impossibility.
3
Read the second argument: act without power is compulsion
Next, Nasafi argues that if an act occurs without the agent having power at the moment, it is by compulsion (idtirār). He illustrates with the example of an act after the loss of physical instruments.
This directly connects the Mu'tazila position to compulsion.
4
Read the third argument: Mu'tazila endorse Jabr
Finally, Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of counting such compelled acts as acts of loyalty to Allah, which is precisely the doctrine of Jabr they condemn. Thus they are guilty of their own accusation.
This completes the refutation, showing the Mu'tazila are the true Jabriyya.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the three arguments build on each other to dismantle the Mu'tazila position. The structure moves from logical impossibility to practical consequence to sectarian identity.
Notice the rhetorical force of turning their own label against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحققه أنها إذا لم تكن موجودة وقت الفعل فلا فرق بين قدرة توجد قبل الفعل وبين قدرة توجد بعد الفعل لاستوائهما في العدم وقت الفعل، والقول (۱) بكونها بعد الفعل محال، فكذا هذا.
ومما يبين سفه المعتزلة وجهلهم بالحقائق أن الفعل لو كان يقع ولا قدرة عليه للفاعل وقت وقوعه لكان وقوعه عن اضطرار، ألا يُرَى أنه لو وقع بعد ارتفاع صحة الآلات لكان واقعًا عن اضطرار، فكذا هذا.
بل أولى إذ حصول الفعل بالقدرة دون صحة الأسباب.
(۲)
ثم إن المعتزلة صيروا العهد بهذا الفصل الواقع عن اضطرار وليا لله تعالى وعدوا له وهذا هو القول بالجبر .
He verifies that if it (power) is not existent at the time of the act, then there is no difference between power that exists before the act and power that exists after the act, because both are equal in non-existence at the time of the act. And the statement that it (exists) after the act is impossible, so likewise this. And among what clarifies the foolishness of the Mu'tazila and their ignorance of realities is that if the act were to occur without the agent having power over it at the time of its occurrence, then its occurrence would be by compulsion. Do you not see that if it occurred after the soundness of instruments had ceased, it would occur by compulsion? So likewise this. Rather even more so, because the occurrence of the act is by power without the soundness of causes. Then the Mu'tazila made the servant—by this section occurring by compulsion—a loyal ally for Allah and counted it for Him, and this is the statement of Jabr.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage contains a multi-part refutation of the Mu'tazila position that power (qudra) must exist before the act, not at the moment of the act. It begins by stating that if power is not present at the time of the act, then whether it existed before or after makes no difference: both are absent at the crucial moment. The claim that power could exist after the act is inherently absurd, so the Mu'tazila's own position collapses. Next, the author argues that if an act occurs without the agent having power at the moment of occurrence, it necessarily occurs by compulsion (idtirār). This is illustrated by the example of an act occurring after the loss of physical capacity—clearly compelled. The situation is even stronger here because acts normally require power, not just sound instruments. The third point directly accuses the Mu'tazila of making the compelled act into an act of loyalty to Allah, which is precisely the essence of Jabr (compulsion) that they themselves condemn. Thus, the Mu'tazila are in fact the true proponents of Jabr.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
اضطرار (idtirār)
جبر (jabr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila position on qudra preserves human free will and agency.
Correction: Imam al-Nasafi demonstrates that their position actually makes all acts compelled, stripping the agent of any real power at the moment of action.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Nasafi, what is the logical consequence of the Mu'tazila claim that power is not present at the time of the act?
A) The act becomes voluntary
B) There is no difference between power before or after the act
C) Power must be re-created at every instant
D) The act becomes impossible

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila view on power leads logically to the very compulsion they condemn, making them the true Jabriyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that denying power at the moment of action reduces humans to mere puppets. In what areas of your life do you live as if your actions are not really yours—attributing them to circumstances, habits, or even Allah's decree? Today, will you take full responsibility for one action you have been avoiding, acknowledging that Allah has given you the power to choose at this very moment?

The Dilemma of Divine Command without Power
Nasafi presents a dilemmatic argument against Mu'tazila on command and prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemmatic argument that, on Mu'tazila principles, divine commands become null and impossible.

Core Idea
If power is absent at the moment of action, divine command becomes impossible.

This passage examines two possibilities regarding when a person can be considered commanded or forbidden under the Mu'tazila view that power is created at the moment of action. Both options lead to absurd consequences that undermine the entire structure of divine law, ultimately describing Allah as unjust or foolish.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Read the passage and notice the two conditional statements starting with 'إن لم يجعلوه' and 'وإن جعلوه'. These represent the two alternatives.
The dilemma is whether the person is considered commanded/forbidden at the time of the act or not.
2
Understand the first horn (not commanded at time of act)
If the person is not considered commanded at the time of the act, the act loses its moral character as obedience or disobedience, nullifying all commands, prohibitions, and Sharia, and making Allah unjust for punishing.
This horn leads to leaving the religion.
3
Understand the second horn (commanded at time of act)
If the person is considered commanded at the time of the act, then Allah commands what the person is incapable of, which is foolish (safah). This also leads to impossibility because command requires capacity.
The elaboration shows command at time of power is ineffective, and command at time of act is impossible.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that on Mu'tazila principles, the entire system of divine obligation collapses, leading to reward and punishment based on frivolity.
This is considered the ugliest disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم إن لم يجعلوه وقت الفصل مأموراً منهيًا بهذا الفعل فقد أخرجوه عن كونه طاعة ومعصية وفاعله عن كونه مطيعا وعاصيًا، وفيه إبطال الأوامر والنواهي ورفع الشرائع وإزالة الحل والحرمة، وهذا خروج عن الملة ودخول في قول منكري الشرائع وإبطال العداوة والولاية، وفيه وصف الله تعالى بكونه ظالما بتعذيبه على فعل من هذه الأفعال، وإن جعلوه وقت الفعل مأمورًا منهيًا، فقد وصفوا الله تعالى بالسفه، حيث أمر بما كان العبد مضطرًا إليه (٤) عاجزا عنه أو نهى عنه. والذي يقرر هذا: أن العبد لو كان مأمورًا بالفعل أو منهيًا عنه وقت وجود والفعل عنه في تلك الحالة مستحيل فكان مأمورًا بما يستحيل وجوده، منهيًا عما يمنع ثبوته، وهذا محال وإن كان مأمورًا وقت القدرة ليفعل بها في الثاني، لم يكن للحال مأمورًا؛ لأن من أمر أن يفعل غدًا لم يكن للحال مأمورًا فلم يكن هو مأمورًا وقت وجود القدرة ، ولا وجه إلى أن يكون مأمورًا وقت وجود الفعل إذ لا قدرة له فى تلك الحال، وتكليف ما لا قدرة له محال وكذا النهي على هذا، فبطل على أصلهم الأمر والنهي وارتفع الحل والحرمة وزالت الشرائع بأسرها، واستحال التكليف، وحصل الوعد والوعيد على العبث والثواب والعقاب على السفه، ومن هذا قوله، فهو واقع في أقبح كفر وأشنع قول - وبالله العصمة عن كل قول هذا عقباه.
Then if they do not make him at the time of the act commanded or forbidden by this act, they have removed it from being obedience or disobedience, and its doer from being obedient or disobedient. And in this is nullification of commands and prohibitions, abrogation of laws, removal of the permitted and forbidden, and this is leaving the religion and entering into the saying of deniers of laws, and nullification of enmity and allegiance, and in it is describing Allah Almighty as unjust by punishing for an act among these acts. And if they make him at the time of the act commanded or forbidden, then they have described Allah Almighty as foolish, where He commanded what the servant was compelled to and incapable of, or forbade him from it. And what establishes this is: if the servant were commanded to perform an act or forbidden from it at the time of existence, while the act is from him impossible in that state, then he would be commanded with what its existence is impossible, forbidden from what prevents its establishment, and this is absurd. And if he were commanded at the time of power to do it in the second moment, he would not be commanded at the present moment, because the one who is commanded to do something tomorrow is not commanded at the present moment, so he is not commanded at the time of existence of power. And there is no way for him to be commanded at the time of existence of the act, since he has no power in that state, and charging with what one has no power over is absurd, and likewise prohibition upon this. So upon their principle, command and prohibition are nullified, the permitted and forbidden are raised, all laws vanish, obligation becomes impossible, and promise and threat become based on frivolity, and reward and punishment upon foolishness. And from this is his statement; so he falls into the ugliest disbelief and the most awful saying. And by Allah is protection from every saying whose consequence is this.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemmatic argument against the Mu'tazila. It considers two possibilities regarding whether a person can be considered commanded or forbidden at the time of the act when, according to the Mu'tazila, power is not present. First option: if they do not consider the person commanded or forbidden at the time of the act, then the act loses its moral character as obedience or disobedience, nullifying all commands and prohibitions, abrogating the Sharia, and resulting in describing Allah as unjust for punishing such acts. This is tantamount to leaving the religion. Second option: if they do consider the person commanded or forbidden at the time of the act, then they ascribe foolishness (safah) to Allah, because He would be commanding what the servant is compelled to and incapable of, or forbidding what is impossible to avoid. The author then elaborates on the second option: command can only apply when the person has the ability to act; if commanded at the time of power (before the act), the command is not effective at the moment of action, so the person is not actually commanded at the moment he acts. If commanded at the time of the act, he has no power, so it is impossible. Thus, on the Mu'tazila's own principle, commands and prohibitions become null and void, religious laws vanish, and the entire system of divine obligation collapses. This leads to the most severe form of disbelief.

Key Terms
أمر (amr)
نهي (nahy)
طاعة (ṭāʿa)
معصية (maʿṣiya)
تكليف (taklīf)
سفه (safah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine commands apply regardless of human capacity.
Correction: This passage shows that commanding without capacity is absurd (foolishness) and unjust; therefore, divine commands must presuppose ability in the subject.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the Mu'tazila consider a person commanded at the time of the act according to Nasafi?
A) The person becomes obedient
B) Allah is described as foolish
C) The act becomes voluntary
D) The command is valid

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila position on human power leads to the impossibility of divine command and the nullification of the entire Sharia.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself making excuses for sin by saying 'I couldn't help it,' are you actually questioning the validity of Allah's command in your life? Repent and seek power from Allah to obey, knowing that He never commands what is impossible.
?
The Mu'tazila thought they were defending Allah's justice, but their view led to describing Allah as foolish. Have you ever held a belief about Allah that seemed well-intentioned but actually implied disrespect? Examine your heart: do you ever assume Allah's commands are too hard for you, thereby implying He is unjust? Commit to trusting His Wisdom in all commands.
?
If divine command became impossible, all obligation and accountability would vanish. Does your daily life reflect a firm belief that Allah's commands are both possible and necessary? Or do you live as if they are optional? Decide today to take one command you've been neglecting and act on it, trusting Allah to give you the ability.

Mu'tazila Objection on Power at the Act and the Reply
Mu'tazila objection and Nasafi's reply on power's existence at the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that power exists at the time of the act and the refutation based on the impossibility of power persisting into the moment of action.

Core Idea
Power cannot simultaneously cause an act and be present at its occurrence; it must cease.

The Mu'tazila try to evade earlier arguments by asserting that power is present exactly when the act occurs. This lesson presents their objection and the reply: power that exists before the act cannot survive into the moment of the act itself. We'll see how this simple logical point upholds the Ash'ari/Maturidi position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and reply
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the exchange.
Notice the concise structure: objection then reply.
2
Analyze the logical move
Notice how the Mu'tazila shift the timing of power to the act itself, and how the reply reasserts the temporal discontinuity.
The reply uses the principle that power cannot persist from one moment to the next.
3
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on why this matters: if power were simultaneous with the act, the act would have two agents, which is theologically problematic.
This refutation is a cornerstone of the Ash'ari/Maturidi stance on human acquisition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن هذا كله إنما يستقيم أن لو كانت القدرة وقت الفعل منعدمة، ونحن لا نقول بذلك بل نقول: إنها موجودة وقت الفعل أيضا.
قيل لهم : إنها إذا وجدت قبل الفصل تنعدم في الثاني الذي هو وقت حصول الفعل، لضرورة استحالة بقائها (٥).
For if they say: All this is only correct if power at the time of the act were non-existent, but we do not say that; rather we say that it exists at the time of the act as well. It is said to them: If it exists before the act, it ceases to exist in the second moment, which is the time of the occurrence of the act, due to the necessity of the impossibility of its persistence.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents a brief objection from the Mu'tazila and the author's reply. The Mu'tazila attempt to evade the previous refutations by asserting that they do not deny power at the time of the act; rather, they claim power exists at that very moment. The author's reply is concise: even if power exists before the act, by the time the act actually occurs, that power has necessarily ceased to exist. Power cannot persist from one moment to the next while the act is happening, because it would then be simultaneously present and absent at the same instant. In other words, the same logical problem recurs: at the precise moment of the act, power is still absent because it has passed away. Thus, the Mu'tazila's objection fails to rescue their position.

Key Terms
قدرة (qudra)
فعل (fi'l)
انعدام (inʿidām)
بقاء (baqāʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If power exists at the time of the act, then the human agent is genuinely acting simultaneously with that power, making the act attributable to the human.
Correction: Power that exists before the act ceases at the moment of the act, so it cannot be simultaneous. The human's power is not present at the act's occurrence, preserving divine causation.

Checkpoint

B) He argues that power before the act ceases to exist at the moment of the act
C) He says power is created by the servant
D) He accepts their claim and changes his argument

Key Takeaway
Power that exists before the act cannot persist into the moment of the act, so the Mu'tazila claim that power exists at the time of the act is untenable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you plan an action, do you ever pause to realize that your ability to perform it vanishes the very moment you need it most? How would your trust in God’s constant creation of your actions change if you truly believed your power ceases before every act?
?
This lesson shows that human power and action cannot coexist. How does that confront your natural feeling of pride after a success? Are you willing to say that your power was already gone by the time the act happened, and that only God’s power was present?
?
The Mu'tazila tried to protect human responsibility by insisting on power at the act. But the reply shows that power and act are separate. What does this teach you about true reliance on God (tawakkul)? Are you willing to act while acknowledging that your ability disappears before the act occurs, and that God alone brings it about?

Power Must Coincide with Action for Obligation
Nasafi argues that without simultaneous power, commands and prohibitions are invalid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Nasafi argues that the Mu'tazilite view of power prior to the act leads to the impossibility of all religious obligations.

Core Idea
If power is not at the moment of action, command becomes impossible.

Nasafi presents a devastating reductio argument against the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) exists before the act. He shows that if power is not simultaneous with the action, then no one can be commanded at the time of action, which would nullify all laws and divine justice. This lesson unpacks that logical chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the conditional chain
Notice how Nasafi starts with a hypothetical: if power is not at the time of action, then... He then lists the consequences.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final claim that this position leads to the 'ugliest disbelief' and why that is a strong rhetorical move.
Nasafi is showing the theological stakes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يكن للحال مأمورًا؛ لأن من أمر أن يفعل غدًا لم يكن للحال مأمورًا فلم يكن هو مأمورًا وقت وجود القدرة، ولا وجه إلى أن يكون مأمورًا وقت وجود الفعل إذ لا قدرة له فى تلك الحال، وتكليف ما لا قدرة له محال وكذا النهي على هذا، فبطل على أصلهم الأمر والنهي وارتفع الحل والحرمة وزالت الشرائع بأسرها، واستحال التكليف، وحصل الوعد والوعيد على العبث والثواب والعقاب على السفه، ومن هذا قوله، فهو واقع في أقبح كفر وأشنع قول - وبالله العصمة عن كل قول هذا عقباه.
He is not commanded at the moment; because whoever is commanded to do something tomorrow is not commanded at the moment, so he is not commanded at the time of the existence of power, and there is no way that he should be commanded at the time of the existence of the action since he has no power in that situation, and obligating that for which there is no power is impossible, and likewise prohibition according to this. So according to their principle, command and prohibition become null, and the lawful and unlawful are removed, and the entire laws disappear, and obligation becomes impossible, and promise and threat become vain, and reward and punishment become foolish, and from this his statement, so he falls into the ugliest disbelief and the most repugnant saying—and by Allah is protection from every saying whose consequence is this.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a reductio ad absurdum argument against the Mu'tazilite position that power (qudra) exists before the act and simultaneously with the act. The author argues that if power does not exist at the exact time of the action, then the agent is not commanded at that time, making the command impossible. This leads to the nullification of all commands, prohibitions, and laws, and ultimately voids reward and punishment, which is absurd and constitutes disbelief. The argument follows a logical sequence: if power precedes the act, then at the moment of the act there is no power, so the agent cannot be held responsible. Hence, the Mu'tazilite position collapses into self-contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies human free will entirely.
Correction: It is specifically a refutation of the Mu'tazilite view of power, not an affirmation of absolute predestination.

D) The agent has free will

Key Takeaway
Nasafi's argument shows that if power is not concurrent with action, the entire framework of religious obligation collapses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
If the entire framework of reward and punishment depends on power being simultaneous with action, then your next moment of choice is crucial. Are you treating your ability to obey as a gift given at that moment, or as something you possess independently? How might this shift your reliance on God?

Refuting Mu'tazilite Objections on Power and Accident Survival
Nasafi refutes three Mu'tazilite objections about power at the time of action and accident survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes Mu'tazilite claims that power can coexist with the action by arguing against accident survival and the possibility of successive powers.

Core Idea
If power precedes the act, it cannot survive; if it coexists, the prior power is useless.

The Mu'tazilites object that power is present at the time of action, challenging the Ash'ari view that power is destroyed before the act. Nasafi responds by first denying accident survival, then showing that even if a new power appears, it either makes the prior power redundant or pushes the problem to the next act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of objections and replies.
Notice the dialectical structure: each objection is followed by a response.
2
Identify the three Mu'tazilite objections
Find each objection: (1) power exists at time of action, (2) power survives, (3) a new power arises successively.
Look for the phrases 'فإن قالوا' marking each objection.
3
Focus on the accident survival argument
Understand why the impossibility of accident survival is key: it forces the destruction of the initial power before the act.
This is the central proof Nasafi relies on.
4
Analyze the dilemma on successive powers
Nasafi asks whether the new power is for this act or a future one. Trace both options to see how each leads to absurdity.
The dilemma shows the Mu'tazilites cannot consistently maintain their position.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the final outcome: the Mu'tazilites either agree with the Ash'aris or affirm a useless power.
This refutation strengthens the argument that power must precede the act without surviving.

فإن قالوا: إن هذا كله إنما يستقيم أن لو كانت القدرة وقت الفعل منعدمة، ونحن لا نقول بذلك بل نقول: إنها موجودة وقت الفعل أيضا. قيل لهم : إنها إذا وجدت قبل الفصل تنعدم في الثاني الذي هو وقت حصول الفعل، لضرورة استحالة بقائها. فإن قالوا: لا نسلم أن بقاءها مستحيل بل هي باقية إلى وقت وجود الفعل فيكون ذلك الفعل بقدرة موجودة ويكون التكليف ثابتا والفاعل قادرا. قيل لهم : قد أقمنا الدلالة على استحالة القول ببقاء الأعراض، وإذا استحال بقاؤها ولم تبق كانت منعدمة في الثاني من زمان وجودها، وذلك هو زمان وجود الفعل. فإن قالوا: إن انعدمت تلك القدرة إلا أن قدرة أخرى تحدث عقيبها إذ القدرة تحدث تباعًا في الصحيح السليم. قيل لهم : تلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني أهي عندكم قدرة هذا الفعل أم قدرة فعل آخر يعقبها؟ وهل يجوز وجود هذا الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة الموجودة في الثاني؟ فإن قالوا: هي قدرة هذا الفعل، ويجوز وجود الفعل بهذه القدرة المقارنة له في الوجود. فقد تركوا مذهبهم، حيث جعلوا الفعل حاصلاً بالقدرة المقارنة له في الوجود، وبقيت القدرة السابقة على الفعل فضلاً لا فائدة في وجودها ولا تعلق لها بفعل ما البتة، وهذا محال فساعدوا أهل الحق في الحقيقة وأثبتوا زيادة لا فائدة فيها، فلم يحصلوا من المخالفة إلا على سفه. فإن قالوا: لا يجوز وجود الفعل في الثاني بهذه القدرة المقارنة له بل وجوده بالقدرة المتقدمة، وتلك القدرة الموجودة في الثاني لفعل يوجد في الثالث لا لفعل يوجد مقارناً لها.
They said: This whole argument is valid only if power is nonexistent at the time of action, but we do not say that; rather we say it exists at the time of action as well. It was said to them: If it exists before the moment, it becomes nonexistent in the second moment, which is the time of the action's occurrence, due to the necessity of the impossibility of its survival. They said: We do not accept that its survival is impossible; rather it continues to exist until the time of the existence of the action, so that action is by an existing power, and obligation is established, and the agent is capable. It was said to them: We have already established the proof of the impossibility of the survival of accidents. If their survival is impossible and they do not persist, they become nonexistent in the second moment of their time of existence, and that is the time of the existence of the action. They said: If that power becomes nonexistent, another power arises after it, since powers arise successively in the sound and healthy person. It was said to them: That power existing in the second moment—is it in your view the power for this action or the power for another action that follows it? And is it permissible for this action to occur in the second moment by this power existing in the second moment? If they say: It is the power for this action, and it is permissible for the action to occur by this power simultaneous with it in existence, then they have abandoned their doctrine, for they have made the action occur by the power simultaneous with it in existence, and the power preceding the action remains a superfluous luxury with no benefit in its existence and no connection to any action at all. This is absurd, so they have agreed with the people of truth in reality and affirmed an addition without benefit, thus they gained nothing from their opposition but foolishness. If they say: It is not permissible for the action to occur in the second moment by this power simultaneous with it; rather its occurrence is by the preceding power, and that power existing in the second moment is for an action that will occur in a third moment, not for an action that occurs simultaneous with it.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange of objections and answers between the author (Nasafi) and the Mu'tazilites. The Mu'tazilites first object that they affirm power at the time of action. The author replies that accidents cannot survive, so if power exists before the act, it must cease to exist at the moment of the act. The Mu'tazilites then assert that power actually survives until the act. The author counters by reiterating the impossibility of accident survival, citing established proof. Next, the Mu'tazilites propose that even if the first power vanishes, another power arises immediately after it, as happens in healthy individuals. The author questions whether this new power is for the same action or a future one. If it is for the same action, then the action is caused by the simultaneous power, contradicting the Mu'tazilite claim that power precedes the act, and leaves the earlier power useless. If it is for a future action, then the action still depends on the earlier power, which is destroyed, leading to the same problem. Thus, the Mu'tazilites are forced into absurdity. The argument proceeds step by step, showing that their position leads to incoherence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Power can continue to exist while bringing about an action simultaneously, without needing to be temporally prior.
Correction: For the action to be due to the agent's power, the power must exist at some time; if it exists before, it must perish before the act (since accidents don't survive), so it cannot cause the act; if it exists at the same time, then it is simultaneous, contradicting the claim of priority, and the prior power is superfluous.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response when the Mu'tazilites claim that a new power arises after the first vanishes?
A) He denies that power can arise
B) He asks whether the new power is for this action or a future one
C) He accepts the objection
D) He says power is eternal

Key Takeaway
Nasafi shows that the Mu'tazilite position on power at the time of action leads to logical contradictions unless they admit the power is simultaneous, which makes their prior power redundant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you act as if your ability to do good or avoid sin comes from your own independent power, or do you constantly acknowledge that Allah creates your actions moment by moment? This lesson shows how even the Mu'tazilites were forced into absurdity when they insisted on independent power. What would change in your life if you truly lived each moment aware that your power is created anew by Allah?
?
The Mu'tazilites held onto a prior power that became redundant—a 'superfluous addition' with no benefit. Are there beliefs, habits, or rituals in your practice of Islam that you cling to despite them adding no real benefit, perhaps out of pride or tradition? What would it cost you to let go of these and submit fully to the truth as taught by Ahl al-Sunnah?
?
When you perform an action, do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency, as if you earned the ability through your own effort? This passage argues that any claim of power preceding the act leads to contradiction. Can you identify a moment today where you will consciously remind yourself that your next action—even turning a page or speaking a word—is only possible by Allah's immediate creation of power? How will you train yourself to maintain that awareness?
