Arguments Against Human Power Extending to Opposites
Al-Nasafi presents proofs from those who deny ability for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key arguments used by those who claim that human power (istita'a) does not extend to opposite actions, and why they distinguish between strength for obedience (tawfiq) and strength for disobedience (khidhlan).

Core Idea
If power could do opposites, then asking for help and refuge would be meaningless.

This passage presents a set of proofs from theologians who deny that human ability can serve both obedience and disobedience. They argue that the strength for each is distinct—tawfiq for obedience, khidhlan for abandonment. These proofs rely on everyday religious practices and logical contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the claim, then a series of proofs.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two types of strength
Find where the passage defines tawfiq and khidhlan. Understand that they are presented as distinct powers.
3
Analyze proofs 1-3: prayer and logical consistency
Examine why asking for help and seeking refuge implies distinct powers. Consider the argument about calling a disbeliever 'protected'.
These proofs rely on common religious language.
4
Consider proof 4 and empirical examples
Look at the argument about combining opposites and the examples of the elderly, sick, etc. What do they show about power?
The examples illustrate that one power cannot do both opposites.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand how this position avoids the Mu'tazilite accusation of taklif ma la yutaq. The passage ends with a note on disagreement among proponents of the second doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الذين يقولون بأن الاستطاعة لا تصلح للضدين وأدلتهم وأما القائلون إنها لا تصلح للضدين وقوة الطاعة غير قوة المعصية يقولون: إن قوة الطاعة التوفيق، وقوة المعصية الخذلان والترك على ما يختار قالوا دليل ذلك أن الناس يسألون من الله العصمة والمعونة على علم منهم أن ليس معه زيغ، ويسألون التوفيق على الإحاطة أن معه الإصابة، ويقولون: اللهم قوني على طاعتك وأعني على أداء ما افترضت على، ويتعوذون بالله من الخذلان، ولو كان بكل واحد منهما ما يكون الآخر لم يكن الذي يسأل بالسؤال أولى من الذي يتعوذ منه، ولو كان بالعصمة زيغ لم يطمئن القلب عند الوجود، فثبت أن قوة كل نوع غير قوة النوع الآخر، ولأن أحدًا لا يطلق القول بأن الكافر معصوم وأن المؤمن مخذول، ولو كان مع كل واحد منهما ما يصلح للأمرين جميعًا لم يكن أحدهما بالشهادة له بالخذلان والآخر بالشهادة له بالتوفيق والعصمة أولى من القلب أو يوصل كل واحد منهما بأنه موفق مخذول وذلك باطل، ولأن الجمع بين الضدين حال وجود القدرة غير ممكن، وهي لا تبقى ليفعل بها ضدًا في وقت والضد الآخر في وقت آخر، فلا يتصور أن تكون قدرة لهما بل كانت قدرة لما وقع بها وكذلك الزمن والعليل والطفل يقدرون على الزحف من الجبل مع العجز عن الارتقاء فيه وكذا العليل ينحدر من السلم على جهد واستمساك ولا يقدر على الارتقاء فيه وكذلك الصحيح ينزل البئر بالجبل ولا يصح منه الارتقاء عنه، وكذا الحي على تفريق أجزاء نفسه ولا يقدر على جمعها، ويقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه الانكسار من الزجاج وغيره من الأجسام، ولا يقدر على السبب الذي يحدث عقيبه ولا الانحيار، وسوى هذا لهم حجج أخر أعرضنا عن ذكرها. ثم إن المذهب الأول يندفع عنه هذا التشنيع الذي تتمسك به المعتزلة وهو نسبة قولنا: إن الاستطاعة الفعل إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق، وأما على مع المذهب الثاني وهو مذهب من يقول: إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين. فقد اختلفت
Those who say that ability does not avail for the two opposites and their proofs. And as for those who say that it does not avail for the two opposites and that the strength of obedience is not the strength of disobedience, they say: The strength of obedience is divine guidance (al-tawfiq), and the strength of disobedience is abandonment (al-khidhlān) and leaving [the servant] to his choice. They said the proof of that is that people ask God for protection and assistance, knowing that there is no deviation with them, and they ask for guidance (al-tawfiq) upon certainty that with it there is correctness, and they say: O God, strengthen me for Your obedience and help me to perform what You have obligated upon me, and they seek refuge with God from abandonment (al-khidhlān). And if each of them had what the other has, then the one who asks would not be more worthy with the question than the one who seeks refuge from it. And if protection had deviation, the heart would not be at ease when it exists. So it is established that the strength of each type is other than the strength of the other type. And because no one says that the disbeliever is protected and that the believer is abandoned. And if each of them had what is suitable for both matters, then one of them would not be more worthy of being witnessed as abandoned and the other as guided and protected than the heart, or [else] each one of them would be described as both guided and abandoned, and that is false. And because the combination of opposites at the time of the existence of power is not possible, and it does not remain so that one opposite is done in one time and the other opposite in another time. So it is inconceivable that it be a power for them; rather it is a power for that which occurs by it. Likewise, the elderly, the sick, and the child are able to descend from a mountain while being unable to ascend it. Likewise, the sick descends from a ladder with effort and holding, and cannot ascend it. Similarly, the healthy descends into a well with a rope and cannot ascend from it. Likewise, a living being is able to separate parts of himself but cannot gather them, and he is able over the cause after which breakage occurs in glass and other bodies, but he is not able over the cause after which non-breakage occurs. And besides this they have other arguments that we have omitted to mention. Then the first doctrine avoids this discrediting that the Mu'tazila cling to, which is attributing our statement that ability is actual ability to the doctrine of imposing what is beyond capacity (taklīf mā lā yuṭāq). But as for the second doctrine, which is the doctrine of those who say that power does not avail for the two opposites, they have differed.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of those who claim that ability (استطاعة) does not extend to opposites, and that the strength for obedience is different from the strength for disobedience. They identify the strength for obedience as divine guidance (التوفيق) and the strength for disobedience as abandonment (الخذلان) and leaving the servant to his choice. The proofs are: 1) People ask God for protection (عصمة) and assistance, knowing that they lack deviation, and they ask for guidance and help in obedience while seeking refuge from abandonment. If each person had both capacities, asking for one and seeking refuge from the other would be pointless. 2) If protection had deviation, the heart would not be at ease. 3) No one would say a disbeliever is protected or a believer is abandoned; if both capacities were present, contradictory descriptions would apply. 4) Combining two opposites at the time of power is impossible, and power does not remain to perform opposites at different times; thus it is only power for the act that occurs. 5) Empirical examples: the elderly can descend but not ascend; the sick can go down a ladder but not up; the healthy can descend a well but not ascend; a person can separate his body parts but not reunite them; one can cause breakage but not prevent it. The passage concludes by noting that the first doctrine (that ability is for opposites) avoids the Mu'tazilite accusation of taklīf mā lā yuṭāq, while the second doctrine leads to disagreement among its proponents.

Key Terms
الاستطاعة
التوفيق
الخذلان
العصمة
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that one power can equally serve both obedience and disobedience, making prayer and descriptions of believers/disbelievers consistent.
Correction: The passage shows that the practice of asking for specific help and the impossibility of contradictory descriptions imply distinct powers for each type of act.

Checkpoint

A) People never ask God for help.
B) The sick can both ascend and descend equally.
C) No one calls a disbeliever 'protected' or a believer 'abandoned'.
D) Power remains after the act to perform another opposite.

Key Takeaway
Theologians argued that ability (istita'a) is specific to one act and does not extend to opposites, as shown by prayer, logic, and everyday examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sincerely ask Allah for strength (tawfiq) to obey Him, do you truly believe that without His specific help you cannot succeed? Or do you subconsciously rely on your own willpower? How does this lesson expose the reality of your dependence on Him in your daily actions?
?
Think of a moment when you felt abandoned (khidhlān) in your worship—when obedience felt impossible. Did you recognize that your inability was not just a lack of effort but a sign of Allah's withdrawal of tawfiq? How does this awareness change the way you respond to spiritual failure?
?
If your power is specific to each act and does not extend to opposites, what does that imply for your plans to obey tomorrow? Do you assume you'll have the same ability then? How does this lesson compel you to constantly seek Allah's help for each single act of obedience, rather than relying on past success?

Nasafi's Refutation of Mu'tazila on Taklif Ma La Yuta
Nasafi argues that Mu'tazila, not Ash'aris, affirm taklif ma la yutaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's argument that the Mu'tazila, not the Ash'aris, are the ones who truly affirm imposing what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila, not the Ash'aris, commit to making every command impossible.

In this passage, Nasafi turns the tables on the Mu'tazilite accusation that Ash'aris affirm taklif ma la yutaq. He argues that if the Mu'tazila examined their own position on capacity, they would see that they are the ones who make every commanded act beyond the agent's ability at the moment of performance. Nasafi's argument hinges on the Mu'tazilite denial of capacity at the time of the act.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the tone: Nasafi is making a counter-accusation.
The passage starts with a conditional: 'If the Mu'tazila looked...'
2
Identify the core claim
Nasafi claims the Mu'tazila are the ones who say taklif ma la yutaq. Find the sentence that states this directly.
It's near the beginning.
3
Understand the argument about capacity
Nasafi explains why: according to the Mu'tazila, capacity does not exist at the time of the act. This makes every command impossible. Follow his reasoning about the two positions on the permanence of accidents.
He covers both possibilities to show the conclusion holds regardless.
4
Contrast with the Ash'ari position
Nasafi states that according to us (Ash'aris), the agent has capacity at the time of the act. So every command is possible. This is the key difference.
5
Reflect on the irony
Nasafi finds it amazing that the Mu'tazila accuse Ash'aris of taklif ma la yutaq when their own position entails it. Consider why this is a strong rhetorical move.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنَّا نقول: إن المعتزلة لو نظروا عن بصيرة ورفضوا التعصب جانبًا وأعرضوا عن تقليد أسلافهم لعرفوا أنهم هم القائلون بتكليف ما لا يطاق وليس في الوسع لا نحن، وذلك لما بينا أن عندنا كان الفاعل وقت الفعل قادرًا وكانت القدرة وقتئذ موجودة، ومن زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على الفعل البتة، إذ هي منعدمة وقت الفعل ولا يتصور وجودها معه، أما على قول من يقول باستحالة بقاء الأعراض فظاهر، وكذا على قول من يقول ببقائها لما بينا أن من زعمهم وإن كانت موجودة كذا الفعل إلا أنها قدرة لغيره من الأفعال ولا يوصف هو بتلك القدرة القائمة للحال أنه قادر على ذلك الفعل بل يوصف بأنه قادر بها على فعل آخر وإنما يوصف بكونه قادرا على هذا الفعل بهذه القدرة قبل وجود الفعل فإنه لا يتصور على قولهم حصول فعل من قادر، ولا قادر يتصور منه الفعل، فكان كل مأمور بفعل مأمورًا بما هو غير قادر عليه وعندنا كل مأمور بما هو قادر عليه ومن أعجب الأقاويل قول من ينسب القائل بأنه كلف ما له عليه القدرة إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق وينسب القائل أنه كلف ما ليس هو بقادر عليه إلى القول
Then we say: If the Mu'tazila looked with insight and abandoned partisanship and turned away from the imitation of their predecessors, they would know that they are the ones who say imposing what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq) and not within capacity, not us. That is because we maintain that the agent at the time of the act is capable and the capacity at that time is existing. But from their claim that there is no capacity for the agent upon the act at all, since it is non-existent at the time of the act and its existence with it is inconceivable. As for the one who says that the permanence of accidents is impossible, it is obvious; likewise for the one who says they are permanent, because we have explained that from their claim, even if it were existing as such, it is a capacity for other acts, and he is not described by that capacity subsisting at the moment as being capable of that act; rather he is described as capable by it of another act. He is only described as being capable of this act by this capacity before the existence of the act. For it is inconceivable according to them that an act occurs from a capable agent, nor can a capable agent from whom the act occurs be conceived. So every one commanded to do an act is commanded to do what he is not capable of. According to us, every one commanded is commanded to do what he is capable of. And among the most amazing sayings is the saying of one who attributes to the one who says that he is commanded to do what he has power over, the statement of imposing what cannot be borne, and attributes to the one who says that he is commanded to do what he is not capable of, the statement [of ...].
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation by the author (Nasafi) against the Mu'tazila. Nasafi opens by stating that if the Mu'tazila examined carefully, they would realize that they, not the Ash'aris, are the ones who affirm imposing what cannot be borne (taklif ma la yutaq). The reason is that according to the Ash'aris, the agent has capacity (qudra) at the time of the act, whereas the Mu'tazila deny capacity at the time of the act. Nasafi explains that according to the Mu'tazila, whether they affirm the permanence of accidents (baqa' al-a'rad) or not, the capacity either does not exist at the time of the act or, if it does, it is a capacity for a different act, not the one being performed. Therefore, the agent is not described as capable of that particular act at the moment it occurs. Consequently, every person commanded to do an act is commanded to do something they are incapable of at that moment, which is exactly taklif ma la yutaq. Nasafi concludes by criticizing those who accuse the Ash'aris of this view while they themselves hold it.

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
القدرة
الفاعل
بقاء الأعراض
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila accuse the Ash'aris of holding that God imposes what cannot be borne.
Correction: Nasafi argues that it is the Mu'tazila themselves whose position on capacity leads to every commanded act being impossible, thus they are the ones who affirm taklif ma la yutaq.

Checkpoint
Why does Nasafi claim the Mu'tazila are the ones who affirm taklif ma la yutaq?
A) They deny that God can command the impossible.
B) They hold that capacity exists at the time of the act.
C) They deny that the agent has capacity at the time of the act, so every command is to the impossible.
D) They believe capacity is created by God.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that the Mu'tazilite denial of capacity at the time of the act logically leads to taklif ma la yutaq, turning their own accusation against them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fail to obey Allah, do you subconsciously blame your lack of ability, thinking it's not your fault because you 'couldn't' do it? After reading this lesson, do you see that your capacity is given by Allah at the very moment of action? Will you now take full responsibility for your obedience and disobedience?
?
Nasafi accuses the Mu'tazila of not looking with insight. Are there areas in your own faith where you cling to a comfortable position without examining its logical consequences? What would it take for you to set aside partisanship and seek the truth, even if it exposes your own errors?
?
If you truly believe that Allah does not command you to do what you are incapable of, how should that change your approach to difficult commandments? Will you start acting on the commands you previously thought were beyond you, trusting that Allah will grant you the capacity at the right moment?

The Mu'tazila's Defense: Distinguishing Two Types of Incapacity
Al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila's distinction between two types of obligation and their defense.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila distinguish between obligating the genuinely impossible and obligating something that becomes impossible due to preoccupation, and how they use this to defend against the charge of taklif ma la yutaq.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila argue that only the second type of obligation incurs imagined punishment, not the first.

The Mu'tazila are accused of holding that God can obligate what is beyond human capacity. In this passage, al-Nasafi presents their counterargument: they distinguish between two kinds of incapacity, and claim that only one involves punishment. This sets the stage for his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila's distinction
Read the Arabic passage where al-Nasafi presents the Mu'tazila's argument. They distinguish between 'obligation of what is impossible' and 'obligation of what is not impossible except that one becomes incapable due to preoccupation.'
Notice the phrases 'المعجوز عنه' and 'ما ليس بمعجوز عنه إلا أنه عجز عنه لاشتغاله بضده'.
2
Identify the role of punishment
See how punishment is only imagined (موهوم) for the second type, not the first.
This is key to their defense.
3
Understand the final justification
The Mu'tazila argue that such obligation can be for subjugation, informing of incapacity, and showing power.
They use this to repel the accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن التكليف إنما يتميز عما ليس بتكليف بهذا قال ولا يتوهم ذلك بغير استحقاق نوع من العقوبة على ضرب من المخالفة فعلى هذا ظهر الفرق له بين تكليف المعجوز عنه وتكليف ما ليس بمعجوز عنه إلا أنه عجز عنه لاشتغاله بضده فإن استحقاق نوع من العقوبة عليه على ضرب من المخالفة موهوم قال: ويجوز ورود ذلك على التسخير والإعلام بالعجز وإظهار القدرة. فعلى هذا يدفعون هذا التشنيع عن أنفسهم.

ثم إنَّا نقول: إن المعتزلة لو نظروا عن بصيرة ورفضوا التعصب جانبًا وأعرضوا عن تقليد أسلافهم لعرفوا أنهم هم القائلون بتكليف ما لا يطاق وليس في الوسع لا نحن، وذلك لما بينا أن عندنا كان الفاعل وقت الفعل قادرًا وكانت القدرة وقتئذ موجودة، ومن زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على الفعل البتة، إذ هي منعدمة وقت الفعل ولا يتصور وجودها معه، أما على قول من يقول باستحالة بقاء الأعراض فظاهر ، وكذا على قول من يقول ببقائها لما بينا أن من زعمهم وإن كانت موجودة كذا الفعل إلا أنها قدرة لغيره من الأفعال ولا يوصف هو بتلك القدرة القائمة للحال أنه قادر على ذلك الفعل بل يوصف بأنه قادر بها على فعل آخر وإنما يوصف بكونه قادرا على هذا الفعل بهذه القدرة قبل وجود الفعل فإنه لا يتصور على قولهم حصول فعل من قادر، ولا قادر يتصور منه الفعل، فكان كل مأمور بفعل مأمورًا بما هو غير قادر عليه وعندنا كل مأمور بما هو قادر عليه ومن أعجب الأقاويل قول من ينسب القائل بأنه كلف ما له عليه القدرة إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق وينسب القائل أنه كلف ما ليس هو بقادر عليه إلى القول بتكليف ما يطاق ولو لم يكن هذا وقاحة أو حماقة فلا وجود لهما إذا، فظهر بهذا أنهم هم القائلون بالجبر المعتقدون بتكليف ما لا يطاق، فتبرؤهم عن ذلك ونسبة خصومهم إلى ذلك جلوس تحت المثل السائر رمتني بدائها وانسلت
Because obligation is distinguished from what is not obligation by this: that it is not imagined except with the deserving of a type of punishment upon a type of violation. So on this basis, the distinction appears for him between the obligation of what is impossible (al-mu'juz 'anhu) and the obligation of what is not impossible except that one becomes incapable of it due to preoccupation with its opposite. For the deserving of a type of punishment upon a type of violation is imagined (mawhum). He said: It is permissible for that to occur for subjugation, informing of incapacity, and showing power. So on this basis they repel this reprehension from themselves.

Then we say: If the Mu'tazila had looked with insight, abandoned partisanship, and turned away from the taqlid of their predecessors, they would have known that they are the ones who say taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of what cannot be borne), not us. That is because we have established that with us, the doer at the time of the act is capable (qadir) and the power (qudra) exists at that time. But from their claim is that the doer has absolutely no power over the act, since it (power) is non-existent at the time of the act and its existence with the act is inconceivable. As for the one who says that accidents cannot endure (istihala baqa' al-a'rad), it is obvious; and likewise for the one who says they endure, because, as we have shown, even if it (power) exists (like the act?) except that it is a power for other acts, and he is not described by that power subsisting in the state as being capable of that act; rather, he is described as being capable by it of another act. He is only described as being capable of this act by this power before the act occurs. For it is inconceivable, on their view, for an act to proceed from a capable agent, and there is no capable agent from whom an act can be conceived. So every one commanded to do an act is commanded to do what he is not capable of. And with us, every one commanded to do an act is commanded to do what he is capable of. And one of the most astonishing statements is the statement of the one who attributes to the one who says that He obligated what he has power over to the saying of taklif ma la yutaq, and attributes to the one who says that He obligated what he is not capable of to the saying of taklif ma yutaq. If this is not insolence or stupidity, then neither exists. So it appears by this that they are the ones who say jabr (compulsion) and believe in taklif ma la yutaq. So their absolving themselves from that and attributing their opponents to that is sitting under the common proverb: 'She threw her own ailment at me and slipped away.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's defense against the charge of taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of what cannot be borne). The author first presents the Mu'tazila's own argument: they distinguish between two types of incapacity—one that is genuinely impossible (al-mu'juz 'anhu) and one that results from preoccupation with an opposite. They claim that punishment is only imagined in the second case, not the first. They use this to argue that God can obligate something that becomes impossible due to the agent's own choice, and they see this as subjugation and demonstration of power. Then al-Nasafi responds: if the Mu'tazila were objective, they would realize that they themselves are the ones who affirm taklif ma la yutaq, not the Ahl al-Sunna. He explains that in the Sunni view, the agent has power (qudra) at the time of the act. In the Mu'tazili view, power is either non-existent at the time of the act (if accidents cannot endure) or, if it exists, it is a power for a different act, not for the act in question. Thus, on their theory, no act can actually proceed from a capable agent; every commanded act is something the agent is not capable of at the moment of the act. This, al-Nasafi argues, is the real taklif ma la yutaq. He then points out the absurdity of their reversing the accusation: they accuse the Sunnis of taklif ma la yutaq when the Sunnis say God obligates what one is capable of, and they accuse the Sunnis of taklif ma yutaq when they say God obligates what one is not capable of. But this is contradictory. Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila are the true determinists (jabriyya) who believe in obligating the impossible, and their accusation against others is like the proverb: 'She threw her own ailment at me and slipped away.'

Key Terms
تكليف ما لا يطاق
معجوز عنه
استحقاق العقوبة
التسخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Mu'tazila deny any form of taklif ma la yutaq, but here they admit it in a qualified sense.
Correction: The Mu'tazila actually accept a form of taklif ma la yutaq when the incapacity results from the agent's own preoccupation, and they justify it as subjugation.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila presented here, when is punishment imagined?
A) For obligating the inherently impossible
B) For obligating what is not impossible but becomes so due to preoccupation
C) For all obligations
D) For no obligations

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila defend themselves by distinguishing between two types of incapacity, claiming that only the second type can involve punishment and can be justified as subjugation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have a duty to pray on time, but sometimes you are 'preoccupied' with work. According to the Mu'tazila's argument, does your preoccupation excuse you? The passage says punishment is 'imagined' even for such cases. How does this make you accountable for your priorities? What will you change today?
?
The Mu'tazila say that God's command can be for subjugation and to show His power, even if you cannot perform it. Have you ever dismissed a divine command as 'impossible for me'? Could it be that God is revealing your own weakness and His power through that command? How will you respond to a difficult command this week?
?
When you fail an obligation, do you usually think it is because it was truly impossible, or because you chose something else? This passage suggests a difference. Reflect on a recent failure: was it due to inherent impossibility or your own preoccupation? What does that mean for your need for repentance and reliance on God?

Al-Nasafi's Refutation: The Mu'tazila Themselves Hold Taklif Ma La Yutaq
Al-Nasafi argues that Mu'tazili theology entails that the agent has no power at the moment of the act, making all obligations effectively impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's argument that, based on the Mu'tazila's own theory of power (qudra), every commanded act is beyond the agent's capacity at the time of the act, making them the true proponents of taklif ma la yutaq.

Core Idea
On Mu'tazili premises, no agent has power at the moment of the act, so all obligations are impossible to fulfill.

Al-Nasafi now turns the tables on the Mu'tazila. He argues that if we examine their own theory of power, we see that they are forced to admit that at the time of the act, the agent possesses no power for that specific act. Consequently, every command is a command to do what is beyond one's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation opening
Al-Nasafi begins by stating that if the Mu'tazila had insight, they would see that they themselves are the ones who say taklif ma la yutaq.
He contrasts with the Sunni position that power exists at the time of the act.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili view of power
Al-Nasafi explains that for the Mu'tazila, power (qudra) is either non-existent at the time of the act (if accidents cannot endure) or, if it exists, it is a power for a different act.
Thus, the agent is never capable of the specific act he is commanded to do at the moment of action.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Therefore, every commanded person is commanded to do what he is not capable of, which is the definition of taklif ma la yutaq.
This is the core of the refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even the Mu'tazila, who pride themselves on rational justice, end up with taklif ma la yutaq. When you have been critical of others' theological positions, have you ever considered that your own assumptions might lead to the very problem you accuse them of? Can you think of an area in your life where your own logic contradicts your stated beliefs? How will you honestly examine that?
?
Al-Nasafi says the Mu'tazila would have known the truth if they abandoned partisanship and taqlid. Think of a personal belief or habit that you hold out of tradition or loyalty to a group, not because you've truly examined it from the sources. What would it cost you to set aside that partisanship and look with 'insight'? Are you willing to do that?
?
The passage emphasizes that at the moment of action, either power exists or it doesn't. In your daily life, when you sin, do you truly feel you had the power to choose otherwise? Or do you, like the Mu'tazila implicitly, believe that you had no real power at that instant? How does the Sunni position on qudra change your sense of responsibility and your need for God's help in the very moment of action?

The Absurd Reversal: Mu'tazila Accuse Sunnis of What They Themselves Do
Al-Nasafi exposes the Mu'tazila's contradictory accusations and declares them the true determinists.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nasafi's exposure of the Mu'tazila's contradictory accusation and his conclusion that they are the true determinists (jabriyya).

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila accuse Sunnis of taklif ma la yutaq for saying God obligates what one can do, which is absurd.

Al-Nasafi points out the ironic reversal: the Mu'tazila accuse the Sunnis of believing in taklif ma la yutaq when the Sunnis affirm that God obligates only what one is capable of, and they accuse them of taklif ma yutaq when they say God obligates what one cannot do. This exposes their prejudice. He concludes that the Mu'tazila are the true compulsionists.

Guided Path
1
Read the final argument
Al-Nasafi presents the astonishing contradiction in the Mu'tazila's accusations.
Notice the reversal: they accuse Sunnis of both extremes.
2
Understand the proverb
Al-Nasafi ends with a proverb: 'She threw her own ailment at me and slipped away.' This means they accuse others of their own fault.
This reinforces the conclusion.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila are the ones who believe in jabr and taklif ma la yutaq.
This flips the common accusation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأن التكليف إنما يتميز عما ليس بتكليف بهذا قال ولا يتوهم ذلك بغير استحقاق نوع من العقوبة على ضرب من المخالفة فعلى هذا ظهر الفرق له بين تكليف المعجوز عنه وتكليف ما ليس بمعجوز عنه إلا أنه عجز عنه لاشتغاله بضده فإن استحقاق نوع من العقوبة عليه على ضرب من المخالفة موهوم قال: ويجوز ورود ذلك على التسخير والإعلام بالعجز وإظهار القدرة. فعلى هذا يدفعون هذا التشنيع عن أنفسهم.

ثم إنَّا نقول: إن المعتزلة لو نظروا عن بصيرة ورفضوا التعصب جانبًا وأعرضوا عن تقليد أسلافهم لعرفوا أنهم هم القائلون بتكليف ما لا يطاق وليس في الوسع لا نحن، وذلك لما بينا أن عندنا كان الفاعل وقت الفعل قادرًا وكانت القدرة وقتئذ موجودة، ومن زعمهم أن لا قدرة للفاعل على الفعل البتة، إذ هي منعدمة وقت الفعل ولا يتصور وجودها معه، أما على قول من يقول باستحالة بقاء الأعراض فظاهر ، وكذا على قول من يقول ببقائها لما بينا أن من زعمهم وإن كانت موجودة كذا الفعل إلا أنها قدرة لغيره من الأفعال ولا يوصف هو بتلك القدرة القائمة للحال أنه قادر على ذلك الفعل بل يوصف بأنه قادر بها على فعل آخر وإنما يوصف بكونه قادرا على هذا الفعل بهذه القدرة قبل وجود الفعل فإنه لا يتصور على قولهم حصول فعل من قادر، ولا قادر يتصور منه الفعل، فكان كل مأمور بفعل مأمورًا بما هو غير قادر عليه وعندنا كل مأمور بما هو قادر عليه ومن أعجب الأقاويل قول من ينسب القائل بأنه كلف ما له عليه القدرة إلى القول بتكليف ما لا يطاق وينسب القائل أنه كلف ما ليس هو بقادر عليه إلى القول بتكليف ما يطاق ولو لم يكن هذا وقاحة أو حماقة فلا وجود لهما إذا، فظهر بهذا أنهم هم القائلون بالجبر المعتقدون بتكليف ما لا يطاق، فتبرؤهم عن ذلك ونسبة خصومهم إلى ذلك جلوس تحت المثل السائر رمتني بدائها وانسلت
Because obligation is distinguished from what is not obligation by this: that it is not imagined except with the deserving of a type of punishment upon a type of violation. So on this basis, the distinction appears for him between the obligation of what is impossible (al-mu'juz 'anhu) and the obligation of what is not impossible except that one becomes incapable of it due to preoccupation with its opposite. For the deserving of a type of punishment upon a type of violation is imagined (mawhum). He said: It is permissible for that to occur for subjugation, informing of incapacity, and showing power. So on this basis they repel this reprehension from themselves.

Then we say: If the Mu'tazila had looked with insight, abandoned partisanship, and turned away from the taqlid of their predecessors, they would have known that they are the ones who say taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of what cannot be borne), not us. That is because we have established that with us, the doer at the time of the act is capable (qadir) and the power (qudra) exists at that time. But from their claim is that the doer has absolutely no power over the act, since it (power) is non-existent at the time of the act and its existence with the act is inconceivable. As for the one who says that accidents cannot endure (istihala baqa' al-a'rad), it is obvious; and likewise for the one who says they endure, because, as we have shown, even if it (power) exists (like the act?) except that it is a power for other acts, and he is not described by that power subsisting in the state as being capable of that act; rather, he is described as being capable by it of another act. He is only described as being capable of this act by this power before the act occurs. For it is inconceivable, on their view, for an act to proceed from a capable agent, and there is no capable agent from whom an act can be conceived. So every one commanded to do an act is commanded to do what he is not capable of. And with us, every one commanded to do an act is commanded to do what he is capable of. And one of the most astonishing statements is the statement of the one who attributes to the one who says that He obligated what he has power over to the saying of taklif ma la yutaq, and attributes to the one who says that He obligated what he is not capable of to the saying of taklif ma yutaq. If this is not insolence or stupidity, then neither exists. So it appears by this that they are the ones who say jabr (compulsion) and believe in taklif ma la yutaq. So their absolving themselves from that and attributing their opponents to that is sitting under the common proverb: 'She threw her own ailment at me and slipped away.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's defense against the charge of taklif ma la yutaq (obligation of what cannot be borne). The author first presents the Mu'tazila's own argument: they distinguish between two types of incapacity—one that is genuinely impossible (al-mu'juz 'anhu) and one that results from preoccupation with an opposite. They claim that punishment is only imagined in the second case, not the first. They use this to argue that God can obligate something that becomes impossible due to the agent's own choice, and they see this as subjugation and demonstration of power. Then al-Nasafi responds: if the Mu'tazila were objective, they would realize that they themselves are the ones who affirm taklif ma la yutaq, not the Ahl al-Sunna. He explains that in the Sunni view, the agent has power (qudra) at the time of the act. In the Mu'tazili view, power is either non-existent at the time of the act (if accidents cannot endure) or, if it exists, it is a power for a different act, not for the act in question. Thus, on their theory, no act can actually proceed from a capable agent; every commanded act is something the agent is not capable of at the moment of the act. This, al-Nasafi argues, is the real taklif ma la yutaq. He then points out the absurdity of their reversing the accusation: they accuse the Sunnis of taklif ma la yutaq when the Sunnis say God obligates what one is capable of, and they accuse the Sunnis of taklif ma yutaq when they say God obligates what one is not capable of. But this is contradictory. Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila are the true determinists (jabriyya) who believe in obligating the impossible, and their accusation against others is like the proverb: 'She threw her own ailment at me and slipped away.'

Key Terms
جبر
تكليف ما لا يطاق
رمتني بدائها وانسلت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Sunnis believe in taklif ma la yutaq, but al-Nasafi argues the opposite.
Correction: The Sunnis believe God obligates only what one is capable of, while the Mu'tazila's own system leads to obligating the impossible.

Checkpoint
What does al-Nasafi say about the Mu'tazila's accusation against the Sunnis?
A) It is accurate and well-founded
B) It is an astonishing contradiction
C) It shows their insight
D) It is based on sound reasoning

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila's accusations against the Sunnis are contradictory and that they themselves are the true believers in jabr and taklif ma la yutaq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself accusing others of a religious error, have you ever paused to examine whether you might be guilty of that same error in a subtler way? How can you protect yourself from the self-deception the Mu'tazila fell into?
?
Al-Nasafi uses sharp language ('insolence or stupidity') against those who contradict themselves in doctrine. How do you respond when you are confronted with a clear inconsistency in your own beliefs? Do you defend, deflect, or humbly reconsider?
?
The proverb 'She threw her own ailment at me and slipped away' describes projecting faults onto others. Is there a belief or practice in your life that you criticize in others but excuse in yourself? What would it take to bring that inconsistency into honest light today?

The Analogy of the Servant and the Saw for Acquired Incapacity
Al-Nasafi answers the objection about power being for one act only and illustrates with an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between excused incapacity (man') and unexcused incapacity (tadyi'), and how al-Nasafi uses this to defend the obligation of faith on the disbeliever.

Core Idea
Incapacity due to wasting is not excused; only prevented incapacity excuses.

Al-Nasafi confronts a key objection to the Ash'ari position that power (qudra) cannot be for two opposites. If a disbeliever lacks power for faith, how can he be obliged? The answer lies in a crucial distinction: incapacity from prevention versus incapacity from wasting. Through a vivid analogy of a servant and a saw, al-Nasafi illustrates that the disbeliever's lack of faith is self-inflicted and thus blameworthy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening sentence where the objection is stated: if power cannot be for opposites, then the disbeliever cannot have power for faith while disbelieving.
The objection is based on the principle of taklif ma la yutaq.
2
Distinguish the two types of incapacity
Identify al-Nasafi's key distinction: man' (prevention) excuses, tadyi' (wasting) does not. The disbeliever falls into the second category.
3
Follow the analogy
Read the two scenarios of the servant and the saw. Notice how the first scenario (locked door) corresponds to prevention, and the second (servant told where key is) corresponds to wasting.
The analogy demonstrates that the disbeliever could have obtained power for faith by intending it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى أن على قول من يقول : إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين لم تكن مع الكافر في حالة كفره قدرة الإيمان.

فنقول : انعدام قدرة الإيمان كان بتضييعه القدرة لاشتغاله بضده لا يمنع القدرة عنه، ومن منع عنه القدرة فهو معذور ، فأما من ضيع القدرة فهو غير معذور بل يعاقب، فإن من أمر عبده بنحت خشب ثم غاب عن العهد ثم جاء والخشب غير منحوت فلامه على ترك النحت فقال العبد : إن القدوم كان في هذا البيت وبابه مقفل، فلم أتمكن من استخراجه والنحت به عذر العبد ولو لم يعذره سيده نسب إلى السفه، ويمثله لو كان قال له: إن القدوم في البيت ومفتاحه في موضع كذا فاذهب وخذ المفتاح وافتح الباب وأخرج القدوم وانحت به ثم غاب السيد ثم حضر ووجد الخشب غير منحوت فلام العبد، فاعتل بأن القدوم في البيت ولم أستخرجه ولم أتمكن من نحته بلا قدوم ، كان للمولى أن يعاقبه على ترك النحت، وإن كان لا قدرة له عليه بدون القدوم إلا أنه تمكن من تحصيله وحيث لم يحصله وبقي على العجز كان ذلك بتضييعه القدرة مع تمكنه من تحصيلها، فكذا فيما نحن فيه أجرى الله تعالى العادة أن من كانت له أعضاء صحيحة وأسباب سليمة لو قصد فعلاً أعطاه قدرة ذلك الفعل، وإنما لم يعطه قدرة فعل لإعراضه عنه مضيعًا للقدرة.
It remains that according to the one who says that power (qudra) is not valid for two opposites: with the disbeliever in his state of disbelief, there would not be power for faith (iman).

We say: The non-existence of the power of faith was due to his wasting the power by occupying himself with its opposite. That does not prevent (the existence of) power from him. But the one from whom power is prevented is excused. As for the one who wasted the power, he is not excused but rather is punished. For example: a man ordered his servant to carve wood, then the master went away for a while, then came back and the wood was not carved, and he blamed the servant for leaving the carving undone. The servant said: 'The saw (al-qadum) was in this house and its door was locked, so I could not take it out and carve with it.' The servant would be excused. If his master did not excuse him, he would be attributed to foolishness. And its example is if he had said to him: 'The saw is in the house and its key is in such-and-such a place; go, take the key, open the door, take out the saw, and carve with it.' Then the master went away, then came and found the wood not carved, and blamed the servant. The servant excuses himself by saying: 'The saw was in the house and I did not take it out, and I could not carve without a saw.' The master would have the right to punish him for leaving the carving, even though he had no power over it without the saw, except that he was able to obtain it. Since he did not obtain it and remained in incapacity, that was due to his wasting the power despite being able to obtain it. So likewise in our case: Allah Almighty has established the custom (al-'ada) that whoever has sound limbs and intact means, if he intends an act, He gives him the power for that act. He only does not give him the power for the act because of his turning away from it, wasting the power.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objection is: if power (qudra) cannot be for two opposites (i.e., if a person has power for one act, he cannot simultaneously have power for its opposite), then how can a disbeliever be required to have faith when he is in a state of disbelief? According to the objection, since he lacks the power for faith at that moment, it would be taklif ma la yutaq. Al-Nasafi responds by distinguishing between two types of incapacity: (1) incapacity due to prevention (man'), which excuses, and (2) incapacity due to wasting (tadyi'), which does not excuse. The disbeliever lacks the power for faith because he has wasted it by occupying himself with its opposite (i.e., disbelief). He is not prevented from it; rather, he failed to avail himself of the means. Al-Nasafi illustrates with a parable of a servant commanded to carve wood. In the first scenario, the saw is locked away and the servant cannot access it — he is excused. In the second scenario, the servant is told where the key is and how to get the saw, but he does not do so — he is not excused because he could have obtained the means. Likewise, Allah has established the sunna (custom) that anyone with sound limbs and intact means who intends an act will be given the power for it. The disbeliever fails to intend faith, so he does not receive the power; his incapacity is self-inflicted and he is accountable. Thus, the obligation of faith on the disbeliever is not taklif ma la yutaq, because he could have obtained the power by intending faith.

Key Terms
قدرة
تضييع القدرة
منع القدرة
معذور
قدوم
أسباب سليمة
أعضاء صحيحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any incapacity excuses from obligation; if someone cannot perform an act, they are not accountable.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that self-induced incapacity (tadyi') does not excuse; only incapacity from prevention (man') excuses.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the two scenarios in the analogy of the servant and the saw?
A) In the first scenario the saw was broken, in the second it was intact.
B) In the first scenario the servant did not know where the saw was, in the second he did.
C) In the first scenario the servant was prevented from accessing the saw, in the second he could obtain it but did not.
D) In the first scenario the master was present, in the second he was absent.

Key Takeaway
The disbeliever's lack of power for faith is due to his own wasting of the opportunity, not due to prevention, so he is accountable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you said 'I can't' to an act of obedience. Was that incapacity truly beyond your control, or was it a waste of the power Allah gave you? What would it take for you to distinguish the two honestly in your own soul?
?
In the parable, the servant knew where the key was but didn’t act. What keys has Allah placed in your life—knowledge, opportunity, health—that you are leaving unused? What would change if you believed that ignoring those keys is a sin?
?
The disbeliever wastes his power by turning away from faith. Where are you turning away from a step toward Allah today? What is the 'saw' you need to reach for, and what excuse are you hiding behind?

The Refutation of the Claim that Inability Excuses the Disbeliever
Nasafi refutes the objection about inability for faith using a master-servant analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why inability resulting from one's own neglect does not excuse a person from obligation, using the master-servant analogy presented by Imam al-Nasafi.

Core Idea
Self-inflicted inability does not excuse; only externally imposed inability does.

Nasafi addresses a key objection in the debate about human ability and divine obligation: if the disbeliever lacks the ability to believe, how can he be blamed? The answer lies in distinguishing between inability that is imposed and inability that results from one's own choice. Nasafi uses a master-servant analogy to clarify this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with an objection, then gives a response with two analogies.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
Locate the opening statement that presents the objection from those who say ability is not suitable for opposites. Then find Nasafi's response that distinguishes between prevention and wasting ability.
The key phrase is 'انعدام قدرة الإيمان كان بتضييعه القدرة'.
3
Understand the two analogies
The passage gives two scenarios: a servant who cannot access the tool and is excused, and a servant who is told how to access it but fails and is punishable. Compare the two to see the difference between imposed and self-inflicted inability.
The first servant says 'القدوم كان في هذا البيت وبابه مقفل' and is excused. The second is told the key's location and fails to retrieve it.
4
Apply the principle to the disbeliever
Nasafi concludes that the disbeliever's inability to believe is due to his own turning away from faith, similar to the second servant. Therefore, he is not excused.
The final sentence explains Allah's custom: ability is given to those who intend an act with sound means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى أن على قول من يقول: إن القدرة لا تصلح للضدين لم تكن مع الكافر في حالة كفره قدرة الإيمان. فنقول: انعدام قدرة الإيمان كان بتضييعه القدرة لاشتغاله بضده لا يمنع القدرة عنه، ومن منع عنه القدرة فهو معذور، فأما من ضيع القدرة فهو غير معذور بل يعاقب، فإن من أمر عبده بنحت خشب ثم غاب عن العهد ثم جاء والخشب غير منحوت فلامه على ترك النحت فقال العبد: إن القدوم كان في هذا البيت وبابه مقفل، فلم أتمكن من استخراجه والنحت به. عذر العبد ولو لم يعذره سيده نسب إلى السفه، ويمثله لو كان قال له: إن القدوم في البيت ومفتاحه في موضع كذا فاذهب وخذ المفتاح وافتح الباب وأخرج القدوم وانحت به ثم غاب السيد ثم حضر ووجد الخشب غير منحوت فلام العبد، فاعتل بأن القدوم في البيت ولم أستخرجه ولم أتمكن من نحته بلا قدوم، كان للمولى أن يعاقبه على ترك النحت، وإن كان لا قدرة له عليه بدون القدوم إلا أنه تمكن من تحصيله وحيث لم يحصله وبقي على العجز كان ذلك بتضييعه القدرة مع تمكنه من تحصيلها، فكذا فيما نحن فيه أجرى الله تعالى العادة أن من كانت له أعضاء صحيحة وأسباب سليمة لو قصد فعلاً أعطاه قدرة ذلك الفعل، وإنما لم يعطه قدرة فعل لإعراضه عنه مضيعًا للقدرة.
It remains that according to the one who says: ability is not suitable for opposites, there would not be with the disbeliever in his state of disbelief an ability for faith. So we say: the non-existence of the ability for faith was due to his wasting the ability by occupying himself with its opposite; that does not prevent the ability from him, and whoever is prevented from ability is excused. But as for the one who wastes ability, he is not excused, rather he is punished. For if a master ordered his servant to carve wood, then was absent during the covenant, then came and the wood was not carved, and blamed him for leaving the carving, and the servant said: 'The adze was in this house and its door was locked, so I could not extract it and carve with it,' the servant is excused, and if his master did not excuse him, he would be attributed to foolishness. And similarly, if he had said to him: 'The adze is in the house and its key is in such a place; go take the key, open the door, bring out the adze, and carve with it,' then the master was absent, then came and found the wood uncarved and blamed the servant, and he excused himself saying that the adze was in the house and I did not extract it and I could not carve without the adze, the master may punish him for leaving the carving. Even though he had no ability for it without the adze, yet he was able to obtain it, and since he did not obtain it and remained in incapacity, that was due to his wasting the ability while being able to obtain it. So likewise in our case, Allah has established the custom that whoever has sound limbs and sound means, if he intends an act, He gives him ability for that act, and He only does not give him ability for an act because of his turning away from it, wasting the ability.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. Nasafi first states the objection: according to those who hold that ability is not suitable for opposites, the disbeliever in his state of disbelief does not possess the ability for faith. Then he gives the answer: the absence of ability for faith is due to the disbeliever wasting his ability by occupying himself with its opposite (disbelief). The one who is prevented from ability by an external factor is excused, but the one who wastes it is not. He illustrates with two analogies: a servant who cannot carve because the adze is locked away is excused; but if the master tells him where the key is and the servant does not retrieve it, he is punishable. Similarly, Allah's custom is that those with sound limbs and means receive ability when they intend an act; failure to receive ability results from turning away and wasting it. Thus the disbeliever is not excused because his inability stems from his own choice to engage in disbelief.

Key Terms
قدرة
تضييع القدرة
تكليف ما لا يطاق
جبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any inability excuses one from obligation.
Correction: Only externally imposed inability excuses; self-inflicted inability due to one's own choice does not.

Checkpoint
According to the analogy in the passage, which servant is punishable?
A) The one whose tool is locked away without him knowing the key's location.
B) The one who is told the tool's location and the key's location but fails to retrieve it.
C) The one who is physically unable to carve.
D) The one who is ordered to carve but has no adze at all.

Key Takeaway
Inability caused by one's own negligence does not excuse one from blame or punishment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you told yourself 'I just can't' about a religious obligation. Using Nasafi's analogy, ask yourself: was the 'adze' truly beyond your reach, or did you know where the key was but chose not to retrieve it? What key have you left unused?
?
If you were to stand before Allah tomorrow and He asked why you neglected a command, would your excuse hold up under the master-servant test? What is one area where you have been blaming circumstances instead of taking ownership of your choices?
?
The disbeliever's failure is described as 'turning away' and 'wasting ability.' In your own life, what small turning away have you allowed to become a pattern? Commit today to one specific step to retrieve the 'adze' you have left in the locked house.

Refuting the Incompatibility of Abilities Argument
Nasafi refutes the incompatibility argument using a paralysis analogy and distinction between prevented and waster.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's response to the claim that the ability for disbelief prevents the ability for faith, using the analogy of paralysis and the distinction between being prevented and being a waster.

Core Idea
The disbeliever is not excused; he wasted his ability through his own choice.

Nasafi addresses an objection from those who argue that because ability cannot be used for opposites, the ability for disbelief blocks faith. He uses a powerful analogy of a paralyzed person to show that the disbeliever is not like the paralyzed: the disbeliever is responsible for his own inability. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the analogy of paralysis.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims that the ability for disbelief prevents faith, just as paralysis prevents punching. Find the three obstacles listed.
The obstacles are ability, will, and disbelief itself.
3
Understand Nasafi's answer
Nasafi distinguishes between being prevented (ma'mun) and being a waster (mudayyi'). The paralyzed is prevented, the disbeliever is a waster. This refutes the objection.
The disbeliever is obligated because he wasted his ability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجاب عن تمويه محققيهم: أن القدرة لما لم تصلح للضدين، وقدرة الكفر لا يتصور حصول الإيمان بها، فكان قيام قدرة الكفر وقيام نفس الكفر ووجود إرادة الكفر من هذا القادر مانعة من وجود قدرة الإيمان كوجود الزمانة والشلل في اليد يكون مانعا من وجود قدرة البطش. ثم التكليف مع الشلل تكليف ما لا يطاق فكذا هذا بل أولى، لأن المانع هناك ثلاثة أشياء: وهي قدرة الكفر وإرادته ونفسه. فإنا نقول: بذلك المانع كان ممنوع القدرة، وبهذه المعاني كان مضيعًا القدرة، فكان ذلك معذوراً ولم يكلف، وبقي هذا مكلفا ولم يقدر على ما ذكرت في المثال. وكما بينت أن في حال وجود الكفر يستحيل وجود الإيمان، وعندهم يؤخذ بذلك الكفر لحصوله باختياره وفوات القدرة بتضييعه وتفويته، ثم صار الحاصل أن لأجل هذه العلة صار التكليف عندنا دائرًا مع صحة الآلات والأسباب دون حقيقة القدرة. لابد لصحة التكليف من سلامة الآلات خلافًا للمعتزلة والأشاعرة. والمعتزلة يشترطون لصحة التكليف حقيقة القدرة، ويموهون أن التكليف بدونها تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، ثم هم القائلون به في التحقيق دون خصومهم. والأشعرية والجبرية لا يشترطون لصحة التكليف لا استطاعة الأسباب والآلات ولا حقيقة الاستطاعة، فألحقت المعتزلة حقيقة القدرة بقدرة سلامة الأسباب لاشتراط التكليف، وألحقت الجبرية استطاعة سلامة الأسباب بحقيقة الاستطاعة في ترك الاشتراط للتكليف، وأصحابنا اشترطوا لذلك سلامة الأسباب والآلات ولم
And with this the obfuscation of their verifiers is answered: that since ability is not suitable for opposites, and the ability for disbelief cannot be conceived as yielding faith, then the presence of the ability for disbelief, the presence of disbelief itself, and the existence of the will for disbelief from this powerful [person] are obstacles to the existence of the ability for faith, like the existence of chronic illness and paralysis in the hand being an obstacle to the existence of the ability for punching. Then obligation with paralysis is an obligation of what is not possible, so likewise this, rather more so, because the obstacle there is three things: the ability for disbelief, its will, and itself. For we say: by that obstacle he was prevented from ability, and by these meanings he was a waster of ability. So that one [the paralyzed] is excused and not obligated, while this one [the disbeliever] remains obligated and is not able [to believe] as mentioned in the example. And as I clarified, in the state of existence of disbelief, the existence of faith is impossible, and according to them he is held accountable for that disbelief because it occurs by his choice, and the loss of ability is due to his wasting and losing it. Then the result is that for this reason obligation in our view revolves around the soundness of instruments and means, not real ability. Soundness of instruments is necessary for the validity of obligation, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the Ash'aris. The Mu'tazila stipulate for the validity of obligation real ability, and they obfuscate that obligation without it is an obligation of what is beyond capacity, yet they in reality are the ones who say it, not their opponents. The Ash'aris and the Jabariyya stipulate for the validity of obligation neither the capability of means and instruments nor real capability. So the Mu'tazila equated real ability with the ability of soundness of means due to stipulating it for obligation, and the Jabariyya equated the capability of soundness of means with real capability in leaving stipulation for obligation, while our masters stipulated for that the soundness of means and instruments, and did not...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument and classification. First, Nasafi addresses an objection: since ability is not suitable for opposites, the ability for disbelief prevents the ability for faith, just as paralysis prevents the ability to punch. The consequence would be that obligation in such a state is impossible. Nasafi answers by distinguishing between being prevented (ma'mun) and being a waster (mudayyi'). The paralyzed person is prevented and excused, but the disbeliever is a waster because he obtained his inability through his own choice. He then summarizes the Maturidi position: obligation depends on soundness of means and instruments, not actual possession of ability. He then contrasts three schools: Mu'tazila require real ability, Ash'aris/Jabariyya require neither, and Maturidis require soundness of means. This highlights the Maturidi middle path: the disbeliever is obligated because his means (limbs, reason) are sound, even though he lacks actual ability due to his own choice.

Key Terms
قدرة
ضدين
زمانة
شلل
تكليف ما لا يطاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disbeliever is excused because he lacks ability, just like a paralyzed person.
Correction: Nasafi distinguishes: the paralyzed is prevented (mamnu') and excused, while the disbeliever is a waster (mudayyi') and remains obligated because his inability is self-inflicted.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, what distinguishes the disbeliever from the paralyzed person?
A) The paralyzed is a waster.
B) The disbeliever is prevented.
C) The paralyzed is excused.
D) Both are excused.

Key Takeaway
The disbeliever is obligated despite lacking ability because he wasted his ability through choice, unlike a paralyzed person who is excused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself falling into a sin repeatedly, do you ever tell yourself 'I just can't help it'? How does Nasafi's distinction between being prevented (excused) and being a waster (accountable) expose the lie behind that excuse? Write down one specific habit you have been excusing and commit to stopping the waste.
?
Think of a command from Allah that you find difficult—like praying on time, lowering your gaze, or giving charity. Have you been treating your difficulty as if you are paralyzed, expecting to be excused? What would it mean to honestly admit that your inability is caused by your own choices and then ask Allah for help?
?
If you truly believed that your wasted abilities make you obligated despite your weakness, how would your relationship with Allah change tonight? Will you repent for the abilities you have wasted and beg Him to restore them, or will you continue making excuses?

The Maturidi Position on Obligation: Soundness of Means
Nasafi presents the Maturidi position on obligation and contrasts it with Mu'tazila and Ash'ari/Jabari views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi view that obligation depends on soundness of means, not real ability, and how it differs from Mu'tazila and Ash'ari/Jabari positions.

Core Idea
Obligation hinges on soundness of means, not on actual ability possessed.

After refuting the argument, Nasafi clarifies the Maturidi principle: taklif (obligation) is valid when the means and instruments are sound, even if the person does not currently possess the ability due to his own fault. He then compares this with the Mu'tazila who require real ability, and the Ash'aris/Jabariyya who require neither. Let's explore this classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the second part of the passage focusing on the Maturidi position and the comparison.
2
Identify the Maturidi condition
Find where Nasafi states that obligation revolves around soundness of means, not real ability.
The phrase 'دائرًا مع صحة الآلات والأسباب' is key.
3
Compare with other schools
Notice how Mu'tazila, Ash'aris, and Jabariyya differ. Mu'tazila require real ability, Ash'aris/Jabariyya require neither.
Maturidis are in the middle.

Response to Objection on Avoiding Sin While Capable
The author answers whether one can avoid sin while having the power to commit it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between instrumental power and real power in relation to sin avoidance.

Core Idea
Instrumental power leaves choice; real power is simultaneous with action.

This passage addresses a common objection about predestination and sin. The opponent asks whether anyone can avoid sin while having the power to commit it. The author's answer hinges on clarifying two different meanings of 'power' (qudra). Let's read the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by an answer with two parts.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the two sentences starting with 'إن عنيت'.
2
Identify the two meanings of power
The author distinguishes 'power of instruments and causes' (قدرة الآلات والأسباب) from 'true power of the action' (حقيقة قدرة الفعل). Find how each is defined and what answer follows.
The first meaning allows avoidance; the second makes the question impossible.
3
Reflect on the impossibility
Consider why the second meaning leads to a contradiction: if 'power' means the power that exists with the act, then asking about avoiding sin while having that power is like asking about avoiding sin while doing it.
This distinction is crucial in Maturidi kalam for understanding human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو قادر عليها؟ : فنقول: إن عنيت بالقدرة قدرة الآلات والأسباب فنعم، وإن عنيت حقيقة قدرة الفعل كان السؤال محالاً، لأن تلك القدرة توجد مع الفعل فكأنك قلت: هل اتقى أحد معصية الله تعالى وهو فاعل لها وهذا محال.
As for their saying: 'Did anyone avoid a disobedience of Allah while he is capable of it?' We say: If you mean by power (qudra) the power of instruments and causes, then yes. But if you mean the true power of the action, then the question is impossible, because that power exists with the action. It is as if you said: 'Did anyone avoid a disobedience of Allah while he is a doer of it?' And this is impossible.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent asks whether anyone can avoid sin while having the power to commit it. The author distinguishes two meanings of 'power' (qudra). 1) Power of instruments and causes (qudrat al-ālāt wa'l-asbāb): this is the capacity to act using means, such as physical ability; in this sense, one can avoid sin while possessing this power. 2) The real power of the action (ḥaqīqat qudrat al-fi'l): this power is simultaneous with the action itself; it is the power that brings the action into existence. If the question means this type, then asking about avoiding sin while having this power is like asking 'Did anyone avoid a sin while he is a doer of it?' which is contradictory and impossible.

Key Terms
قدرة الآلات والأسباب
حقيقة قدرة الفعل
معصية
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a person has power over an action, he must commit it.
Correction: The author clarifies that instrumental power does not necessitate the action; one can have the ability to sin and still avoid it.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the answer to the question 'Did anyone avoid a sin while capable' if the power meant is the power of instruments?
A) No, it is impossible.
B) Yes, one can avoid sin with instrumental power.
C) The question is invalid regardless.
D) It depends on the person.

Key Takeaway
The author resolves a theological objection by distinguishing between instrumental power and the power that is co-extensive with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a temptation to sin, do you tell yourself 'I have the power to do it, so I might as well'? How does this lesson challenge that thought?
?
Think of a sin you have committed in the past. Was your power to commit that sin the same as the power that existed at the moment of the act? How does distinguishing between these two powers affect your sense of responsibility?
?
If you know you have the instrumental ability to avoid a sin, what is actually holding you back? Is it your own choice, or are you waiting for a feeling of 'true power' that may never come without action?

The Counter-Dilemma on Power and Obedience
The author presents a counter-dilemma to the opponent's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi author uses a counter-dilemma to force the opponent to accept the simultaneity of power and obedience.

Core Idea
A clever counter-dilemma can expose an opponent's inconsistency.

In this brief but powerful passage, the author turns the tables on the opponent. Instead of directly answering the previous objection, he poses a parallel dilemma that forces the opponent to either accept a distressing consequence or concede the Maturidi position. This rhetorical move illustrates the dialectical skill in kalam arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic text once and then the translation. Notice the structure: a question followed by two possible answers.
2
Identify the Dilemma
The question asks whether Allah gives the power of obedience exactly at the same time as the obedience. Identify the two options for the opponent.
The options are 'No' and 'Yes'.
3
Understand Each Consequence
If 'No', a great distress becomes possible. If 'Yes', the opponent admits the Maturidi statement. Reflect on what these consequences imply.
The 'great distress' likely means the impossibility of performing obedience without simultaneous power.
4
Reflect on the Dialectical Power
Consider why this is an effective counter-objection: it mirrors the opponent's own method and forces them into a tight corner.
This shows the importance of anticipating objections in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقابل بمثل هذا التشنيع فيقال: هل أعطى الله تعالى وليا قوة الطاعة بعين الطاعة ؟ فإن قال : لا ، تمكنت وحشة عظيمة، وإن قال : نعم، أقر بقولنا.
Then he is confronted with a similar disparagement, and it is said: Did Allah give a saint the power of obedience coincident with obedience itself? If he says: No, a great distress becomes possible. And if he says: Yes, he admits our statement.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a counter-objection. After the opponent's question, the author now poses a similar challenge to the opponent. The question is: Did Allah give a saint (walī) the power of obedience exactly in the moment of obedience (bi-‘ayn aṭ-ṭā‘a)? If the opponent says 'No'—meaning the power is not simultaneous with obedience—then a serious problem arises (wahsha ‘aẓīma), likely referring to the impossibility of performing obedience without power. If the opponent says 'Yes'—that Allah does give power coincident with obedience—then the opponent concedes the Maturidi position that power and action are simultaneous.

Key Terms
قوة الطاعة
بعين الطاعة
ولي
وحشة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This counter-objection is a genuine doubt or problem for the Maturidi.
Correction: It is a dialectical tool to expose inconsistency in the opponent's position.

Checkpoint
What is the implication if the opponent says 'No' to the question about giving the power of obedience coincident with obedience?
A) The opponent admits our position.
B) A great distress becomes possible.
C) The opponent is proven wrong.
D) The power is given separately.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi author uses a mirrored counter-dilemma to force the opponent into either accepting an absurdity or conceding the simultaneity of power and obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken credit for your own obedience, forgetting that the very power to obey was given to you by Allah in that exact moment? How does this lesson expose your pride?
?
When you fail to obey, do you blame yourself entirely or do you recognize that Allah withholds the power? How can admitting your complete dependence on Allah in every act change the way you approach worship?
?
This argument uses a dilemma to corner an opponent. But consider: You are your own opponent when you argue internally against obeying a command. Which side of the dilemma do you fall into—denying God's role and so feeling distressed, or admitting that every good act is from Him? What commitment does this demand of you today?

When Inability Does Not Excuse Disobedience
Objection-and-answer distinguishing prevention from waste of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi refutation of the claim that inability excuses disobedience, specifically the distinction between being prevented from having power and wasting power by choosing an alternative.

Core Idea
Inability excuses only when prevented, not when power is wasted by free choice.

One of the most persistent objections to the doctrine of divine justice and human responsibility is the claim that inability should always excuse disobedience. After all, if someone cannot do something, how can they be blamed? In this passage, the author of the Tabṣira addresses this very objection by making a crucial distinction. By the end of this lesson, you will see how the Maturidi response preserves both divine justice and human accountability.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: the opponents' claim is followed by the author's response.
The passage has an objection-and-answer format common in kalam.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find the part where the opponents argue that in the seen world, the best excuse is 'I could not do it.' Understand why they think this applies to divine commands as well.
Their claim is based on analogy from the seen to the unseen.
3
Spot the author's distinction
Look for the two cases the author distinguishes: being prevented from having power versus wasting power by preferring an alternative. This is the core of the refutation.
The Arabic word 'ḍayya‘ahā' means 'wasted it' – key term.
4
Understand the clarification about blame
Notice that the author says blame is not for non-existence of the commanded act but for doing the opposite. This further sharpens the point.
The opposite act was done with power, so the servant is accountable.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how this distinction resolves the objection and upholds the Maturidi position on human responsibility.
The author ends by noting that these arguments suffice to answer any further doubts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم من أن يقول : لو قيل له لم لا فعلت كذا ؟ فيقول: لأنني لم أقدر عليه، فمثله في الغائب . قلنا : هذا يكون عذرًا فيما منع عنه القدرة لا فيما ضيعها بإيثاره بدله. على أنه لا يعاتب على انعدام المأمور به من قبله بل لفعله ضد المأمور به وقد فعل ذلك عن قدرة. وفي المسألة شبهات كثيرة للخصوم ومن أحكم ما أوردناه لا يتعذر عليه الانفصال عما يورد عليه من تلك الشبهات - إن شاء الله تعالى.
For they said: There is no excuse for a servant in the seen world greater than his saying, when it is said to him 'Why did you not do such-and-such?', he says: 'Because I was not capable of it.' So its like applies to the unseen. We said: This is an excuse in what he is prevented from power over, not in what he wasted by preferring its opposite. Moreover, he is not blamed for the non-existence of the commanded act from his side, but for his doing the opposite of the commanded act, and he did that by power. And in the issue there are many doubts for the opponents, and whoever comprehends what we have presented will not find it difficult to separate himself from those doubts that are presented - Allah willing.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The opponent argues that in the seen world, the best excuse is 'I could not do it,' so similarly in the unseen (regarding divine commands), inability should be an excuse. The author replies by distinguishing two cases: (1) When a person is prevented from having power over the action (muna‘a ‘anhu al-qudra), then inability is a valid excuse. (2) When the person has power but wastes it (ḍayya‘ahā) by choosing an alternative (bi-īthārihi badalahu), then it is not an excuse. The author further clarifies that the servant is not blamed for the mere non-occurrence of the commanded act (because he might be unable) but for actually doing the opposite act, which he performed with power (qadara). The passage concludes by noting that while there are many doubts, the arguments given are sufficient to resolve them.

Key Terms
عذر
شاهد
غائب
ضيع
إيثار بدل
عاتب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any inability to perform a commanded act is a valid excuse for disobedience.
Correction: Inability is only an excuse when the person is prevented from having the power to perform the act. If the person has power but wastes it by choosing an alternative, then inability is not an excuse.

Checkpoint
When is inability a valid excuse according to the author?
A) When the person does not have the instruments.
B) When the person is prevented from having power over the action.
C) Whenever the person claims inability.
D) When the person does not commit the act.

Key Takeaway
Inability excuses only when the person is prevented from having the power, not when they waste their power by voluntarily choosing an alternative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a sin you have committed while knowing it was wrong. Can you honestly claim you were 'prevented' from doing the right, or did you waste your ability by choosing the easier alternative? What will you do differently next time you face that choice?
?
When you fail to perform an obligatory act, do you immediately excuse yourself by saying 'I couldn't'? This lesson demands you examine whether you had the power but wasted it. Which specific area of your life have you been excusing, and what step will you take today to use your power for obedience?
?
Consider a habit you justify as 'too hard to break.' According to this lesson, if you have the power to choose otherwise, your excuse is invalid. What concrete change will you commit to, starting now, to stop wasting your power?
