The Creation of Human Actions: Jabriyya and Mu'tazila Views
Nasafi introduces the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila positions on the creation of human actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two extreme positions on human actions—the Jabriyya's compulsion (jabr) and the Mu'tazila's complete human agency (ikhtiyār)—and the key concepts of power (qudra), metaphorical attribution (majāz), and creation (takhlīq).

Core Idea
The debate on human actions hinges on whether humans have real power or are mere metaphors for God's acts.

After establishing the concept of capacity (istiṭāʿa), Imam Nasafi turns to the central question: Are human actions created for humans or for God? He presents the Jabriyya and Mu'tazila views as the two extremes that the Maturidi position will reconcile. In this lesson, we examine these two opposing views and their foundational claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Note that the passage ends mid-sentence; the continuation will be in later lessons.
2
Identify the two groups and their core claims
The passage presents two views: that of Jahm and his followers (Jabriyya) and that of the Mu'tazila (Qadariyya). Find the key sentence that introduces each view.
Look for 'قول جهم وأتباعه بالجبر' and 'قول المعتزلة بالاختيار'.
3
Understand the Jabriyya's metaphorical attribution of actions
According to the Jabriyya, actions are entirely compulsory (iḍṭirāriyya) like involuntary tremors. Attributing actions to humans is merely metaphorical (majāz), as when we say 'Zayd died' or 'the boy grew tall'—these are descriptions, not acts of agency.
The text gives examples: 'ذهب زيد' (Zayd went) is like 'مات زيد' (Zayd died).
4
Understand the Mu'tazila's claim of human invention of actions
The Mu'tazila held that humans invent their actions, bringing them from non-existence into existence, regardless of God's will. Earlier Mu'tazila avoided calling humans 'creator' but still affirmed the meaning of creation.
The text says: 'يخترعها العباد فيخرجونها من العدم إلى الوجود'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث

Chapter Three: Speech on the creation of the actions of servants. Now that we have finished the exposition of capacity (al-istiṭāʿa), its affirmation, its divisions, and the time of establishment of each of them, we proceed to discuss the actions of servants: whether they are created for them or for God? So we say—and with God is success: The statement of Jahm and his followers on compulsion (al-jabr): People differed concerning the actions of creation. Some of them made them [belong] to God, and they denied from them the governance of creation and removed from them their power, rather they did not affirm for them any power, and made them entirely compulsory (iḍṭirāriyya) like the movements of a trembling person and the movements of pulsing veins, and they deemed impossible the attribution of power (qudra) to servants—and this is the statement of Jahm bin Ṣafwān al-Tirmidhī—and attribution of actions to servants according to these [people] is metaphorical (majāz). So the saying of one who says: 'Zayd went' and 'Amr walked' is like the saying of one who says: 'The boy grew tall' and 'Zayd died' and 'ʿUmar's hair turned white' and 'ʿAbdullāh grew old.' The statement of the Muʿtazila on choice (al-ikhtiyār): And some of them made them [actions] [belong to] the servants and cut off God's governance from them entirely, and said: The servants invent them, bringing them from non-existence into existence and producing them, and they say: Their bringing into existence and production [happens] whether God wills that or not. To [this view] went the Qadariyya Muʿtazila in their entirety, except that their early ones used to be cautious about uttering the word 'creation' (at-takhlīq) and naming the servant a 'creator' (khāliqan), and they used to conform with all Muslims in their saying: 'There is no creator but God,' although they affirmed the meaning of creation, they used to utter...
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on the creation of human actions. Nasafi signals that he has finished discussing capacity (istiṭāʿa) and now turns to the central question: Are human actions created for humans, or for God Almighty? He then presents two opposing positions. The first is the view of Jahm bin Ṣafwān and his followers, the Jabriyya. They held that all actions belong to God alone, deny any effective power or governance to humans, and assert that humans have no power (qudra) at all. Actions are entirely compulsory (iḍṭirāriyya), like involuntary tremors or pulse beats. Attributing actions to humans is merely metaphorical (majāz), akin to saying 'Zayd died' or 'the boy grew tall'—these are descriptions, not acts of agency. The second view is that of the Muʿtazila (Qadariyya). They held that actions truly belong to humans and that God's governance is entirely cut off from them. Humans invent their actions, bringing them from non-existence to existence. They assert that these actions occur whether God wills them or not. Nasafi notes that earlier Muʿtazila were reluctant to use the word 'creation' (takhlīq) for humans or call a human 'creator', but they still affirmed the meaning. The passage ends mid-sentence, implying that Nasafi continues by stating that these earlier Muʿtazila used to say 'There is no creator but God' while still meaning that humans create in a qualified sense. The passage sets up the debate that the author will engage with.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Nasafi endorses one of these views presented here.
Correction: Nasafi is presenting these as extreme positions to be refuted in favor of the Maturidi middle path.

Checkpoint

B) As metaphorical (majāz)

D) As dependent on both God and human will

Key Takeaway
The two extreme views on human actions—Jabriyya's compulsion and Mu'tazila's complete human agency—set the stage for the Maturidi middle path, which affirms human capacity while recognizing God as the ultimate creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Jabriyya liken human actions to involuntary tremors, do you ever catch yourself excusing your sins as if they were inevitable, as if you had no choice? How does that mental shift—from responsible agent to passive victim—undermine your sense of accountability before Allah? Commit today to stop blaming your circumstances and own your choices fully.
?
The Mu'tazila claimed that human actions occur whether God wills them or not. Is there an area of your life where you act as if Allah's will is irrelevant—where you believe your success or failure depends entirely on you? Where in your heart have you silently asserted independence from His will? Confess that area right now and consciously submit it back to His decree.
?
This passage forces you to confront a tension: either your actions are not really yours (Jabriyya), or Allah has no real role in them (Mu'tazila). Which extreme does your heart naturally lean toward when you reflect on your own life? Are you more prone to fatalism or to self-reliance? Pause and recognize how both tendencies can subtly erode your tawheed. Make a firm intention to seek the balanced path that gives Allah His due while taking responsibility for your deeds.

The Shared False Premise of Jabariyya and Mu'tazila
Explains the false premise that led to both Jabariyya and Mu'tazila errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how both the Jabariyya and the Mu'tazila started from the same false premise yet arrived at contradictory conclusions.

Core Idea
Both schools erred because they assumed one act cannot be under two powers.

This passage reveals a surprising common ground between two seemingly opposite theological schools. The Jabariyya (extreme determinists) and the Mu'tazila (extreme libertarians) both began with the same false assumption: that a single action cannot be governed by both divine and human power. From there, each group selectively emphasized one side of the truth, leading to their characteristic errors. Understanding this shared root helps us see why their conclusions are equally flawed and why the moderate position avoids their mistake.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the structure: it first states the common false premise, then traces the Mu'tazila path, then the Jabariyya path.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مقدمة كاذبة' — this is key.
2
Identify the false premise
What is the false premise that both groups agreed upon? It is stated clearly: that one object of power cannot be under the power of two agents. Find the Arabic phrase and confirm the translation.
3
Trace the Mu'tazila reasoning
The Mu'tazila focused on proofs of human agency. From the false premise, they concluded that human actions must be outside divine power. Note how they turned a blind eye to proofs of divine omnipotence.
4
Trace the Jabariyya reasoning
The Jabariyya focused on proofs of divine omnipotence. From the false premise, they denied any real power to servants, even making all actions compulsory. Understand the logical steps: if God's power covers an act, no other power can attach to it; therefore, no other power exists.
The author explains why a power that cannot attach to any object is impossible—it would be no different from incapacity.
5
Reflect on the corrective
The author implicitly points to a middle path that affirms both divine power and human agency without the false premise. Consider how the Ash'ari or Maturidi position resolves this by allowing one act to be under two powers in different respects.
This lesson does not detail the solution, but plants the seed for later study.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما تفرع هذان المذهبان الباطلان المتناقضان أعني مذهب الجبرية ومذهب القدرية الحاصلين على طرفي الغلو والتقصير من اتفاق الفريقين على مقدمة كاذبة، وهى أن دخول مقدور واحد تحت قدرة قادرين محال، لما وجدوا ذلك في الشاهد محالاً، والشاهد أصل الغائب، فبعد ذلك نظرت المعتزلة إلى الدلائل الموجبة كون العباد فاعلين قادرين فتمسكوا بها، وجعلوا أفعال العباد الداخلة تحت قدرتهم مخلوقة لهم خارجة عن قدرة الله تعالى (وعموا عن الدلائل التي توجب إحالة خروج مقدور عن قدرة الله تعالى) . ونظرت الجبرية إلى الدلائل الموجبة دخول هذه الأفعال تحت قدرة الله تعالى المثبتة إحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق لغير الباري جل وعلا، فتمسكوا بتلك الدلائل وجعلوا الأفعال مخلوقة لله تعالى خارجة عن أن تكون مقدوره لغيره لاستحالة تعلق قدرة غير الله تعالى بما تعلقت به قدرة الله تعالى ثم لما لم يتصور عندهم مقدور لا تعلق له بقدرة الله تعالى لا يتصور أن يتعلق مقدور ما بمقدور غير الله تعالى ولو كانت لغير الله تعالى قدرة لكانت قدرة لا يتصور تعلقها بمقدور ما البتة، ومن المحال وجود قدرة لا يتصور تعلقها بمقدور إذ لم يكن حينئذ بينهما وبين العجز فرق، أبوا على هذه القضية أن تكون لغير الله تعالى قدرة، وجعلوا أفعال الحيوانات كلها اضطرارية، وعموا عن الدلائل الموجبة أن يكون للعباد فعل وأن لهم القدرة على أفعالهم.
And these two false contradictory schools, namely the school of the Jabariyya and the school of the Qadariyya, who ended up at the two extremes of exaggeration and deficiency, only branched off from the agreement of the two groups on a false premise: that the entry of a single object of power under the power of two powerful agents is impossible. Because they found that in the witnessed world impossible, and the witnessed is the origin of the unseen. After that, the Mu'tazila looked at the proofs indicating that servants are agents and powerful, so they held onto them and made the actions of servants that fall under their power created by them, outside the power of Allah, and they turned a blind eye to the proofs that require the impossibility of any object of power being outside the power of Allah. And the Jabariyya looked at the proofs requiring the entry of these actions under the power of Allah, which establish the impossibility of establishing the power of creation for other than the Exalted Allah, so they held onto those proofs and made the actions created by Allah, outside being an object of power for other than Him, due to the impossibility of the power of other than Allah being attached to what the power of Allah is attached to. Then, since in their view no object of power is conceivable that has no attachment to the power of Allah, it is inconceivable that any object of power attaches to the power of other than Allah. And if there were a power for other than Allah, it would be a power that cannot attach to any object of power at all, and the existence of a power that cannot attach to an object of power is impossible, for then there would be no difference between it and incapacity. So they refused, on this basis, that there be a power for other than Allah, and they made all actions of animals compulsory, and turned a blind eye to the proofs that require that servants have action and that they have power over their actions.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a critique of both the Jabariyya (determinists) and the Qadariyya (Mu'tazila libertarians), identifying the common false premise that led to their errors. The author states that both schools agreed on a false premise: that a single object of power (maqdūr) cannot be under the power (qudra) of two agents, based on analogy from the witnessed world (shāhid) to the unseen (ghā'ib). From this premise, each group took a different path. The Mu'tazila focused on proofs that servants are agents, so they concluded that actions are created by the servant and outside divine power, ignoring proofs of divine omnipotence. The Jabariyya focused on proofs that actions are under divine power, so they denied any power to the servant, even making all actions compulsory (iḍṭirārī). The author explains the Jabariyya's reasoning: if an action were under God's power, it cannot be under another's power; therefore, no other power can exist, because a power that cannot attach to any object is impossible (it would be like incapacity). Thus they denied any power for the servant and turned a blind eye to proofs of human agency. The passage highlights the one-sidedness of both groups.

قدرية
اضطرارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that Jabariyya and Mu'tazila are polar opposites with no common ground.
Correction: The passage shows they both agreed on a false premise—that one object of power cannot be under two powers—which led to their errors. They share a fundamental mistake.

Checkpoint
What was the common false premise that both the Jabariyya and the Mu'tazila agreed upon?
A) Humans have no free will
B) A single object of power cannot be under the power of two agents
C) God creates all actions directly
D) Humans create their own actions independently

Key Takeaway
The error of both extremist schools stems from a false premise about the impossibility of sharing power over a single act, ignoring the possibility of different kinds of power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you struggle with questions of free will and God's control, do you find yourself leaning toward one extreme? What false premise about God's power or your own might be driving that tendency? Will you repent of that assumption today?
?
The Mu'tazila clung to some proofs and ignored others. Is there any area of your faith where you are so convinced by one set of evidence that you have turned a blind eye to clear proofs that would lead you to the balanced truth? Name it now.
?
The false premise seemed 'obvious' from the witnessed world. What is one 'obvious' assumption you have about God or yourself that might actually be false because it is based on limited human experience? Commit to re-examining it with the Qur'an and sunnah.

The Ahl al-Sunna Position on Human Actions and Divine Power
States the Ahl al-Sunna position on human actions and divine power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ahl al-Sunna affirms both human agency and divine creation of actions, and why this establishes divine justice and bounty.

Core Idea
Affirming human actions while recognizing they are created by Allah establishes justice.

This passage presents the balanced position of Ahl al-Sunna on one of the most debated theological issues: human actions and divine power. The author explains that Ahl al-Sunna fully affirm that humans truly perform actions and thereby become obedient or disobedient, yet these actions are created by Allah. This resolves the tension between human responsibility and divine omnipotence, leading to the affirmation of divine justice and bounty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the list of affirmations and negations.
2
Identify the key affirmations and negations
Find where the author states that Ahl al-Sunna affirmed actions, made them created, negated impossibility of two powers, and negated compulsion.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how from the first part (affirmation of human actions) comes justice, and from the rest comes divine bounty.
The Quranic verse supports the justice point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من حقق الأفعال للخلق وقال : إنهم بها صاروا عصاة مطيعين، وجعلوها مخلوقة الله تعالى، ونفوا إحالة تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد، ونفوا الضرورة عن أفعالهم، وبدخولها تحت قدرة الباري جل وعلا، فيستفاد بالأول ثبوت العدل ونفي الظلم تحقيقا لقوله تعالى: وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ () وإثبات الفضل ﴿
And among them is one who affirmed actions for the creatures and said: By them they became disobedient and obedient, and he made them created by Allah, and he negated the impossibility of two powers attaching to a single object of power, and he negated compulsion regarding their actions, and their entry under the power of the Exalted Allah. So from the first is derived the establishment of justice and the negation of injustice, in realization of His saying: And your Lord is not unjust to His servants ( ) and the affirmation of bounty.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the correct school (madhhab) of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. The author begins by stating that the Ahl al-Sunna 'affirmed actions for the creatures' (ḥaqqaqa al-af‘āl li-l-khalq), meaning they fully recognized that human beings truly perform actions and thereby become obedient or disobedient. However, they also affirmed that these actions are 'created by Allah' (makhliqat Allāh ta‘ālā). They rejected the idea that it is impossible for two powers (qudratān) to attach to a single object of power (maqdūr wāḥid). They also rejected 'compulsion' (ḍarūra) regarding human actions, meaning they are not forced like involuntary movements. The actions 'enter under the power of the Exalted Allah' (bi-dukhūlihā taḥta qudrat al-Bārī), meaning divine power encompasses them. The result is that by affirming human agency (the first part), justice is established: the servant deserves reward or punishment, and there is no injustice, as the Quran says, 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants' (Surah Fussilat 41:46). Additionally, divine bounty (al-faḍl) is affirmed, meaning God's favor in guiding and rewarding.

Key Terms
تحقيق الأفعال
عصاة مطيعين
مخلوقة
قدرتين بمقدور واحد
الضرورة
العدل
نفي الظلم
الفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that affirming human actions necessarily denies divine creation, or that affirming divine creation denies human agency.
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna affirm both: humans truly act, but their actions are created by Allah, avoiding both extremes.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna as presented in this passage, what is the relationship between human actions and divine power?
A) Actions are created by humans and outside divine power
B) Actions are created by Allah but humans truly perform them
C) Actions are compulsory with no human role
D) Humans create their actions and God does not create them

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna maintains a balanced position: humans truly act, but their actions are created by Allah, establishing both divine justice and bounty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you take—every sin and every good deed—is created by Allah, yet you are fully responsible for it? How does this truth change the way you think about your failures and your repentance? Will you begin each day by acknowledging both your accountability and your utter dependence on Allah?
?
If your obedience were solely due to Allah's grace and not your own merit, would you still strive to obey? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart? This week, when you do something good, pause and silently attribute it to Allah's bounty, then thank Him.
?
Do you ever blame your circumstances or Allah's decree for your disobedience, falling into the trap of Jabariyya? In your next moment of sin, stop and remind yourself: 'This action is my choice, and Allah created it.' How will that change your path to repentance?

Refuting the Jabriyya on Human Actions
Nasafi presents the Jabriyya's argument and their error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Jabriyya's argument for denying human power and why it is flawed.

Core Idea
The Jabriyya wrongly conclude that divine power excludes all human power.

In this passage, al-Nasafi critiques the Jabriyya, who overemphasized Allah's power to the point of denying any real human action. He explains their reasoning and shows how they ignored proofs for human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the Jabriyya's argument.
Notice that the argument starts with a premise about Allah's power encompassing all actions.
2
Identify the Jabriyya's reasoning steps
Break down their argument: (1) Allah's power covers all actions, (2) No other power can attach to the same action, (3) If another power existed, it would be useless, (4) Therefore, no power for creatures.
This reasoning is based on a misunderstanding of how divine and human power relate.
3
Spot the flaw in their reasoning
The Jabriyya ignored proofs that humans have real actions and power. The author criticizes them for being 'blind' to these proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الداخلة تحت قدرتهم مخلوقة لهم خارجة عن قدرة الله تعالى وعموا عن الدلائل التي توجب إحالة خروج مقدور عن قدرة الله تعالى. ونظرت الجبرية إلى الدلائل الموجبة دخول هذه الأفعال تحت قدرة الله تعالى المثبتة إحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق لغير الباري جل وعلا، فتمسكوا بتلك الدلائل وجعلوا الأفعال مخلوقة لله تعالى خارجة عن أن تكون مقدوره لغيره لاستحالة تعلق قدرة غير الله تعالى بما تعلقت به قدرة الله تعالى. ثم لما لم يتصور عندهم مقدور لا تعلق له بقدرة الله تعالى لا يتصور أن يتعلق مقدور ما بمقدور غير الله تعالى ولو كانت لغير الله تعالى قدرة لكانت قدرة لا يتصور تعلقها بمقدور ما البتة، ومن المحال وجود قدرة لا يتصور تعلقها بمقدور إذ لم يكن حينئذ بينهما وبين العجز فرق، أبوا على هذه القضية أن تكون لغير الله تعالى قدرة، وجعلوا أفعال الحيوانات كلها اضطرارية، وعموا عن الدلائل الموجبة أن يكون للعباد فعل وأن لهم القدرة على أفعالهم. [مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة] ومنهم من حقق الأفعال للخلق وقال : إنهم بها صاروا عصاة مطيعين، وجعلوها مخلوقة الله تعالى، ونفوا إحالة تعلق قدرتين بمقدور واحد، ونفوا الضرورة عن أفعالهم، وبدخولها تحت قدرة الباري جل وعلا، فيستفاد بالأول ثبوت العدل ونفي الظلم تحقيقا لقوله تعالى: وَمَا رَبُّكَ بِظَلَّامٍ لِلْعَبِيدِ وإثبات الفضل تحقيقا لقوله تعالى: وَلَوْلَا فَضْلُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْكُمْ وَرَحْمَتُهُ . ويستفاد بالثاني معرفة بأن الله تعالى موصوف بما وصف به نفسه محمود به کما قال تعالى خَلِقُ كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَكيل . هذا هو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة.
Those [actions] that fall under their power are created by them, outside the power of Allah Almighty. And they were blind to the proofs that necessitate the impossibility of a thing of power (maqdur) leaving the power of Allah Almighty. And the Jabriyya looked at the proofs that necessitate the entry of these actions under the power of Allah Almighty, which establish the impossibility of proving the power of creation (takhliq) for other than the Creator, may He be exalted. So they clung to those proofs and made actions created by Allah Almighty, outside of being a thing of power for other than Him, due to the impossibility of the attachment of the power of other than Allah to that to which the power of Allah has attached. Then, since in their view there is no conceived thing of power that has no attachment to the power of Allah, it is inconceivable that any thing of power should attach to a power of other than Allah. And if there were a power for other than Allah, it would be a power whose attachment to any thing of power is inconceivable at all, and the existence of a power whose attachment to a thing of power is inconceivable is impossible, for then there would be no difference between it and incapacity. So they denied, on this basis, that there is a power for other than Allah, and they made all the actions of animals compulsory, and they were blind to the proofs that necessitate that servants have action and that they have power over their actions. [The School of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a] And among them are those who affirmed actions for creatures and said: by them they become disobedient and obedient, and they made them created by Allah Almighty, and they negated the impossibility of the attachment of two powers to one thing of power, and they negated compulsion from their actions, while affirming their entry under the power of the Creator, may He be exalted. So by the first [affirmation of action for creatures] the establishment of justice and the negation of oppression is benefited, as a realization of His saying: 'And your Lord is not unjust to His servants' [41:46], and the affirmation of grace as a realization of His saying: 'And were it not for the grace of Allah upon you and His mercy' [24:10]. And by the second [affirmation of creation by Allah] the knowledge that Allah Almighty is described with what He described Himself, praised thereby, is benefited, as He said: 'Creator of everything, and He is over everything a Guardian' [39:62]. This is the school of Ahl al-Sunna wal-Jama'a.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Jabriyya position followed by a presentation of the correct Sunni doctrine. The author begins by referencing a previous statement about a group (likely the Mu'tazila) that claimed actions under human power are created by them and lie outside Allah's power, and notes that they were blind to proofs showing the impossibility of any thing of power escaping Allah's power. Then the author turns to the Jabriyya, who looked at the proofs that all actions are under Allah's power and concluded that actions are exclusively created by Allah and cannot be under any other's power. The author explains their reasoning: since Allah's power attaches to all actions, it is impossible for another power to attach to the same action, because if another power existed, it would be a power that cannot attach to anything, which is indistinguishable from incapacity. Therefore, they deny any power for creatures and make all actions compulsory, ignoring the proofs that affirm real human action and power. The author then presents the Sunni school: they affirm actions for creatures (so that they become obedient or disobedient through them) while also affirming that Allah creates those actions. They deny the impossibility of two powers attaching to one action and deny compulsion. From the first affirmation (human action) comes the benefit of establishing justice and negating oppression, as in the verse 'Your Lord is not unjust to His servants,' and also establishing grace, as in 'were it not for the grace of Allah.' From the second affirmation (divine creation) comes the benefit of knowing Allah as He describes Himself, as the Creator of everything. The author concludes that this is the Sunni doctrine.

Key Terms
الجبرية
القدرة
المقدور
التخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jabriyya think that divine power and human power are mutually exclusive; if Allah creates all actions, humans cannot have real power.
Correction: The Sunni doctrine shows that two powers can attach to one action: Allah creates, humans acquire. This is not contradictory.

Checkpoint
What is the core premise of the Jabriyya's argument as presented in this passage?
A) Humans have power over their actions
B) Allah's power attaches to all actions
C) Two powers cannot attach to one action
D) Actions are created by humans

Key Takeaway
The Jabriyya erred by denying human power based on a flawed understanding of divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—even the sins you struggle with—is entirely created by Allah, yet you are fully responsible for choosing them? How does this truth make you feel accountable right now? Will you commit to seeking His help while knowing you have real choice?
?
The Jabriyya used proofs of divine power to deny their own responsibility. In what areas of your life do you use Allah's decree as an excuse to avoid taking responsibility for your actions? What is one specific step you will take today to act as if your choices matter?
?
If you knew that your power to act is real but limited, and that Allah's power is absolute but not tyrannical, how would that change the way you pray for strength to obey Him? Will you now ask Him to help you use the power He has granted you?

Divine Decree
intermediate
5 min
Sunni Doctrine: Affirming Human Action and Divine Creation
Nasafi states the Sunni doctrine on human actions and its two theological benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni position that humans have real actions and power while Allah creates those actions, and the theological benefits of this view.

Core Idea
Humans act, Allah creates; justice and grace are thus established.

Having refuted the Jabriyya, al-Nasafi now presents the correct Sunni doctrine. He explains that by affirming both human action and divine creation, we uphold divine justice, reject oppression, and recognize Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Sunni position
Read the passage and note the two affirmations: humans have real actions (they become obedient/disobedient), and Allah creates those actions.
Also note that they negate the impossibility of two powers and negate compulsion.
2
Identify the two affirmations and their benefits
The first affirmation (human action) leads to justice and grace. The second (divine creation) leads to knowledge of Allah as Creator.
These benefits are grounded in Quranic verses.
3
Connect to the overall theological framework
This position balances human responsibility with divine sovereignty, avoiding both Jabriyya (compulsion) and Qadariyya (excessive free will).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if humans have real power, then Allah is not the sole creator of actions.
Correction: The Sunni view holds that humans perform actions while Allah creates them, so divine sovereignty is maintained alongside human responsibility.

Checkpoint
What two benefits are derived from the Sunni position on human actions?
A) Establishment of divine mercy and human free will
B) Establishment of divine will and negation of human responsibility
C) Establishment of justice and negation of oppression, and knowledge of Allah as Creator
D) Establishment of human power and negation of divine power

Key Takeaway
The Sunni view harmonizes human responsibility with divine sovereignty by affirming both human action and divine creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every action you perform—even your obedience and disobedience—is created by Allah? If so, how does that affect your sense of responsibility and your reliance on His grace?
?
When you sin, do you blame yourself or do you feel that Allah created that sin and therefore it is His fault? How does the Sunni doctrine of two powers attaching to one action correct that mindset?
?
If your salvation depends entirely on Allah's grace, not on your own actions, how does that change the way you approach worship and repentance today? Will you commit to thanking Allah for His grace every time you do good?
